	\newpage
	
	\section{Lattice Structures}
	
	\subsection{Irreducibility and Primality}
	
	\begin{defn}
		Let $(L, \leq)$ be a poset.
		\begin{itemize}
			\item Suppose that $(L, \leq)$ is a join semilattice.
			\begin{itemize}
				\item Let $x \in L$. Then $x$ is said to be
				\begin{itemize}
					\item \tbf{$\leq$-join irreducible} if 
					\begin{enumerate}
						\item $x$ is not a zero of $L$,
						\item for each $a,b \in L$, $x = a \vee_{\leq} b$ implies that $x = a$ or $x = b$ 
					\end{enumerate}
					\item \tbf{$\leq$-join prime}
					\begin{enumerate}
						\item $x$ is not a zero of $L$,
						\item if for each $a,b \in L$, $x \leq a \vee_{\leq} b$ implies that $x \leq a$ or $x \leq b$
					\end{enumerate}
				\end{itemize}
				\item We define 
				\begin{itemize}
					\item $\irrj(L, \leq) \defeq \{x \in L: \text{$x$ is $\leq$-join irreducible.}\}$
					\item $\prmj(L, \leq) \defeq \{x \in L: \text{$x$ is $\leq$-join prime.}\}$
				\end{itemize}
			\end{itemize}
			\item Suppose that $(L, \leq)$ is a meet semilattice. 
			\begin{itemize}
				\item Let $x \in L$. Then $x$ is said to be 
				\begin{itemize}
					\item \tbf{$\leq$-meet irreducible} 
					\begin{enumerate}
						\item $x$ is not a one of $L$,
						\item if for each $a,b \in L$, $x = a \wedge_{\leq} b$ implies that $x = a$ or $x = b$ 
					\end{enumerate}
					\item \tbf{$\leq$-meet prime} if
					\begin{enumerate}
						\item $x$ is not a one of $L$,
						\item for each $a,b \in L$, $a \wedge_{\leq} b \leq x$ implies that $a \leq x$ or $b \leq x$
					\end{enumerate}
				\end{itemize}
				\item We define 
				\begin{itemize}
					\item $\irrm(L, \leq) \defeq \{x \in L: \text{$x$ is $\leq$-meet irreducible.}\}$
					\item $\prmm(L, \leq) \defeq \{x \in L: \text{$x$ is $\leq$-meet prime.}\}$
				\end{itemize}
			\end{itemize}
		\end{itemize}
	\end{defn}
	
	\begin{note}
		When the context is clear, 
		\begin{itemize}
			\item we say ``join irreducible", ``join prime", ``meet irreducible" and ``meet prime" in place of ``$\leq$-join irreducible", ``$\leq$-join prime", ``$\leq$-meet irreducible" and ``$\leq$-meet prime" respectively,
			\item we write $\irrj(L)$, $\prmj(L)$, $\irrm(L)$ and $\prmm(L)$ in place of $\irrj(L, \leq)$, $\prmj(L, \leq)$, $\irrm(L, \leq)$ and $\prmm(L, \leq)$ respectively
		\end{itemize}
	\end{note}
	
	\begin{ex}
		Let $(L, \leq)$ be a poset. 
		\begin{enumerate}
			\item Suppose that $(L, \leq)$ is a join semilattice. Then $\prmj(L) \subset \irrj(L)$. 
			\item Suppose that $(L, \leq)$ is a meet semilattice. Then $\prmm(L) \subset \irrm(L)$. 
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{ex}
	
	\begin{proof}\
		\begin{enumerate}
			\item Let $x \in \prmj(L)$.
			\begin{enumerate}
				\item Since $x$ is join prime, $x$ is not at zero of $L$.
				\item Let $a,b \in L$. Suppose that $x = a \vee b$. Then $x \leq a \vee b$. Since $x$ is join prime, $x \leq a$ or $x \leq b$. 
				\begin{itemize}
					\item Suppose that $x \leq a$. Then 
					\begin{align*}
						a \vee b
						& = x \\
						& \leq a \\
						& \leq a \vee b
					\end{align*}
					and therefore $x = a$. Thus $x \leq a$ implies that $x = a$.
					\item Similarly, $x \leq b$ implies that $x = b$. 
				\end{itemize}
				Since $x \leq a$ or $x \leq b$, we have that $x = a$ or $x = b$. Since $a,b \in L$ with $x = a \vee b$ are arbitrary, we have that for each $a,b \in L$, $x = a \vee b$ implies that $x = a$ or $x = b$.
			\end{enumerate} 
			Thus $x \in \irrj(L)$. Since $x \in \prmj(L)$ is arbitrary, we have that for each $x \in \prmj(L)$, $x \in \irrj(L)$. Hence $\prmj(L) \subset \irrj(L)$.
			\item Similar to $(1)$ \tcr{use duality}. 
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{proof}
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	\subsection{Lattice Ideals and Filters}
	
	\begin{defn}
		Let $L$ be a lattice.  
		\begin{itemize}
			\item Let $J \subset L$. Then
			\begin{itemize}
				\item $J$ is said to be an \tbf{ideal of $L$} if 
				\begin{enumerate}
					\item $J \neq \varnothing$,
					\item for each $a,b \in J$, $a \vee b \in J$,
					\item for each $x \in L$ and $x \in J$, $x \wedge a \in J$,
				\end{enumerate}
				\item $J$ is said to be an \tbf{filter of $L$} if 
				\begin{enumerate}
					\item $J \neq \varnothing$,
					\item for each $a,b \in J$, $a \wedge b \in J$,
					\item for each $x \in L$ and $a \in J$, $x \vee a \in J$.
				\end{enumerate}
			\end{itemize}
			\item We define 
			\begin{itemize}
				\item $\MI(L) \defeq \{I \subset L: \text{$I$ is an ideal of $L$}\}$
				\item $\MF(L) \defeq \{F \subset L: \text{$F$ is a filter of $L$}\}$
			\end{itemize}
		\end{itemize}
	\end{defn}
	
	\begin{ex}
		Let $L$ be a lattice. If $L \neq \varnothing$, then 
		\begin{enumerate}
			\item $L \in \MI(L)$,
			\item $L \in \MF(L)$.
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{ex}
	
	\begin{proof}\
		\begin{enumerate}
			\item 
			\begin{enumerate}
				\item Since $L$ is a lattice, $L \neq \varnothing$.
				\item Clearly for each $a,b \in L$, $a \vee b \in L$
				\item Clearly for each $x \in L$ and $b \in L$, $a \wedge b \in L$
			\end{enumerate}
			\item Similar to $(1)$ \tcr{or use dualtity?}.
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{proof}
	
	\begin{ex}
		Let $L$ be a lattice. Then for each $x \in L$,
		\begin{enumerate}
			\item $\doar x \in \MI(L)$
			\item $\upar x \in \MF(L)$
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{ex}
	
	\begin{proof}
		Let $x \in L$.
		\begin{enumerate}
			\item 
			\begin{enumerate}
				\item Since $x \in \doar x$, $\doar x \neq \varnothing$.
				\item Let $a,b \in \doar x$. Then $a \leq x$ and $b \leq x$. Hence $x \in \ubd(\{a,b\})$. Therefore 
				\begin{align*}
					a \vee b
					& = \sup \{a,b\} \\
					& \leq x.
				\end{align*}
				Thus $a \wedge b \in \doar x$. Since $a,b \in \doar x$ are arbitrary, we have that for each $a,b \in \doar x$, $a \vee b \in \doar x$.
				\item Let $y \in L$ and $a \in \doar x$. Then $a \leq x$. Therefore 
				\begin{align*}
					y \wedge a
					& = \inf( \{y, a\}) \\
					& \leq a \\
					& \leq x.
				\end{align*}
				Hence $y \wedge a \in \doar x$. Since $y \in L$ and $a \in \doar x$ are arbitrary, we have that for each $y \in L$ and $a \in \doar x$, $y \wedge a \in \doar x$.
			\end{enumerate}
			\item \tcr{use duality}
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{proof}
	
	\begin{ex}
		Let $L$ be a lattice and $J \subset L$. Set $\leq_J \defeq \leq|_{J^2}$. Then
		\begin{enumerate}
			\item $J \in \MI(L)$ iff $J$ is a $\leq$-lower set and $(J, \leq_J)$ is upward directed. \tcr{rework after making some exercises about subprosets, subdirected sets and subposets and showing facts about $\sup \/ \inf$ of subprosets and upper/lower sets in sub-prosets}  
			\item $J \in \MF(L)$ iff $J$ is an $\leq$-upper set and $(J, \leq_J)$ is downward directed.
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{ex}
	
	\begin{proof}\
		\begin{enumerate}
			\item 
			\begin{itemize}
				\item $(\implies)$: \\
				Suppose that $J \in \MI(L)$.
				\begin{itemize}
					\item Let $x \in L$ and $a \in J$. Suppose that $x \leq a$. Then $x = \inf(\{a,b\}, \leq)$ and therefore
					\begin{align*}
						x
						& = \inf(\{x, a\}, \leq) \\
						& = x \wedge_{\leq} a \\
						& \in J. 
					\end{align*}  
					Since $a \in J$ and $x \in L$ with $x \leq a$ are arbitrary, we have that $J$ is a down set. 
					\item Since $J \in \MI(L)$, we have that $J \neq \varnothing$. Let $a, b \in J$. Since $J \in \MI(L)$, 
					\begin{align*}
						\sup (\{a,b\}, \leq) \\
						& = a \vee_{\leq} b \\
						& \in J.
					\end{align*}
					Hence \tcr{(ref ex here)}  
					\begin{align*}
						\sup (\{a,b\}, \leq)
						& = \sup (\{a,b\}, \leq_J) \\
						& \in \ubd(\{a,b\}, \leq_J)
					\end{align*}
					and $\ubd(\{a,b\}, \leq_J) \neq \varnothing$.
					Since $a,b \in J$ are arbitrary, we have that for each $a,b \in J$, $\ubd (\{a,b\}, \leq_J) \neq \varnothing$. Since $J \neq \varnothing$ and for each $a,b \in J$, $\ubd(J, \leq_J) \neq \varnothing$, we have that $(J, \leq_J)$ is upward directed.
				\end{itemize}
				\item $(\impliedby)$: \\
				Suppose that $J$ is a $\leq$-lower set and $(J, \leq_J)$ is upward directed. 
				\begin{enumerate}
					\item Since $(J, \leq_J)$ is upward directed, $J \neq \varnothing$.
					\item Let $a,b \in J$. Since $(J, \leq_J)$ is upward directed, $\ubd(\{a,b\}, \leq_J) \neq \varnothing$. Thus there exists \tcr{(ref ex here)} 
					\begin{align}
						c 
						& \in \ubd(\{a,b\}, \leq_J) \\
						& \subset \ubd(\{a,b\}, \leq).
					\end{align}
					Therefore $\sup(\{a,b\}, \leq) \leq c$. Since $J$ is a $\leq$-lower set, $c \in J$ and $\sup(\{a,b\}, \leq) \leq c$, we have that 
					\begin{align*}
						a \vee b
						& = \sup(\{a,b\}, \leq) \\
						& \in J.
					\end{align*}
					Since $a,b \in J$ are arbitrary, we have that for each $a,b \in J$, $a \vee b \in J$.
					\tcr{rework after making some exercises about subprosets, subdirected sets and subposets and showing facts about $\sup \/ \inf$ of subprosets and upper/lower sets in sub-prosets}
					\item Let $x \in L$ and $a \in J$. Then $x \wedge a \leq a$. Since $J$ is a $\leq$-lower set, $x \wedge a \in J$. Since $x \in L$ and $a \in J$ are arbitrary, we have that for each $x \in L$ and $a \in J$, $x \wedge a \in J$.
				\end{enumerate}
				Therefore $J \in \MI(L)$.
			\end{itemize}
			\item 
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{proof}
	
	\begin{ex}
		Let $L$ be a lattice and $J \subset L$.
		\begin{enumerate}
			\item If $L$ has a zero and $J \in \MI(L)$, then $0 \in J$.
			\item If $L$ has a one and $J \in \MF(L)$, then $1 \in J$.
		\end{enumerate} 
	\end{ex}
	
	\begin{proof}\
		\begin{enumerate}
			\item Suppose that $L$ has a zero and $J \in \MI(L)$. Since $J$ is an ideal of $L$, $J \neq \varnothing$ and \tcr{a previous ex} implies $J$ is a lower set. Hence there exists $a \in J$. \tcr{a prev ex} implies that $0 \in \lbd L$. Thus $0 \leq a$. Since $J$ is a lower set, $0 \in J$.
			\item Similar to $(1)$.
		\end{enumerate} 
	\end{proof}
	
	\begin{ex}
		Let $L$ be a lattice. 
		\begin{enumerate}
			\item Let $(I_{\al})_{\al \in A} \subset \MI(L)$. 
			\begin{enumerate}
				\item If $\bigcap\limits_{\al \in A} I_{\al} \neq \varnothing$, then $\bigcap\limits_{\al \in A} I_{\al} \in \MI(L)$. 
				\item If $A = \{1,2\}$, then $\bigcap\limits_{\al \in A} I_{\al} \in \MI(L)$.
				\item If $L$ has a zero, then $\bigcap\limits_{\al \in A} I_{\al} \in \MI(L)$.
			\end{enumerate}
			\item Let $(F_{\al})_{\al \in A} \subset \MF(L)$. 
			\begin{enumerate}
				\item If $\bigcap\limits_{\al \in A} F_{\al} \neq \varnothing$, then $\bigcap\limits_{\al \in A} F_{\al} \in \MF(L)$. 
				\item If $A = \{1,2\}$, then $\bigcap\limits_{\al \in A} F_{\al} \in \MF(L)$.
				\item If $L$ has a one, then $\bigcap\limits_{\al \in A} F_{\al} \in \MF(L)$. 
			\end{enumerate}
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{ex}
	
	\begin{proof} \
		\begin{enumerate}
			\item Set $I \defeq \bigcap\limits_{\al \in A} I_{\al}$.
			\begin{enumerate}
				\item Suppose that $I \neq \varnothing$.
				\begin{enumerate}
					\item By assumption $I \neq \varnothing$.
					\item Let $a, b \in I$ and $\al \in A$. Then $a,b \in I_{\al}$. Since $I_{\al} \in \MI(L)$, $a \vee b \in I_{\al}$. Since $\al \in A$ is arbitrary, we have that for each $\al \in A$, $a \vee b \in I_{\al}$. Hence $a \vee b \in I$.
					\item Let $x \in L$, $a \in I$ and $\al \in A$. Then $a \in I_{\al}$. Since $I_{\al} \in \MI(L)$, $x \in L$ and $a \in I_{\al}$, we have that $x \wedge a \in I_{\al}$. Since $\al \in A$ is arbitrary, we have that for each $\al \in A$, $x \wedge a \in I_{\al}$. Hence $x \wedge a \in I$. Since $x \in L$ and $a \in I$ are arbitrary, we have that for each $x \in L$ and $a \in I$, $x \wedge a \in I$. 
				\end{enumerate}
				Thus $I \in \MI(L)$.
				\item Suppose that $A = \{1,2\}$. 
				\begin{enumerate}
					\item Since $I_1, I_2 \in \MI(L)$, $I_1 \neq \varnothing$ and $I_2 \neq \varnothing$. Thus there exist $x_1 \in I_1$ and $x_2 \in I_2$. Since $I_1 \in \MI(L)$, $x_2 \in L$ and $x_1 \in I_1$, we have that $x_1 \wedge x_2 \in I_1$. Similarly, $x_1 \wedge x_2 \in I_2$. Hence $x_1 \wedge x_2 \in I_1 \cap I_2$ and $I_1 \cap I_2 \neq \varnothing$. Part $1(a)$ implies that $I_1 \cap I_2 \in \MI(L)$.
				\end{enumerate}
				\item Suppose that $L$ has a zero. Let $\al \in A$. Since $I_{\al} \in \MI(L)$, \tcr{a previous exercise} implies that $0 \in I_{\al}$. Since $\al \in A$ is arbitrary, we have that for each $\al \in A$, $0 \in I_{\al}$. Hence $0 \in I$. Thus $I \neq \varnothing$. The previous part implies that $I \in \MI(L)$.
			\end{enumerate}
			\item Similar to $(1)$.
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{proof}
	
	\begin{ex}
		Let $L$ be a lattice. Then 
		\begin{enumerate}
			\item for each $I_1, I_2 \in \MI(L)$, $\{x \in L: \text{there exist $a \in I_1$ and $b \in I_2$ such that $x \leq a \vee b$} \} \in \MI(L)$.
			\item for each $F_1, F_2 \in \MF(L)$, $\{x \in L: \text{there exist $a \in F_1$ and $b \in F_2$ such that $a \vee b \leq x$} \} \in \MF(L)$ \tcr{(maybe FIX!!!)}.
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{ex}
	
	\begin{proof}\
		\begin{enumerate}
			\item Let $I_1, I_2 \in \MI(L)$. Set $I \defeq \{x \in L: \text{there exist $a \in I_1$ and $b \in I_2$ such that $x \leq a \vee b$} \}$. 
			\begin{enumerate}
				\item Since $I_1, I_2 \in \MI(L)$, we have that $I_1 \neq \varnothing$ and $I_2 \neq \varnothing$. Hence there exist $x_1 \in I_1$ and $x_2 \in I_2$. Since $x_1 \leq x_1 \vee x_2$, we have that $x_1 \in I$. Thus $I \neq \varnothing$. 
				\item Let $a,b \in I$. Then there exist $x_a, x_b \in I_1$ and $y_a, y_b \in I_2$ such that $a \leq x_a \vee y_a$ and $b \leq x_b \vee y_b$. Define $z_1, z_2 \in L$ by $z_1 \defeq x_a \vee x_b$ and $z_2 \defeq y_a \vee y_b$. Since $I_1, I_2 \in \MI(L)$, we have that $z_1 \in I_1$ and $z_2 \in I_2$. We note that
				\begin{align*}
					a 
					& \leq x_a \vee y_a \\
					& \leq (x_a \vee y_a) \vee (x_b \vee y_b) \\
					& = (x_a \vee x_b) \vee (y_a \vee y_b) \\
					& = z_1 \vee z_2.
				\end{align*}
				and similarly $b \leq z_1 \vee z_2$. Therefore $z_1 \vee z_2 \in \ubd(\{a, b\}, \leq)$. Hence
				\begin{align*}
					a \vee b
					& = \sup(\{a, b\}, \leq) \\
					& \leq z_1 \vee z_2.
				\end{align*}
				Thus $a \vee b \in I$. Since $a,b \in I$ are arbitrary, we have that for each $a,b \in I$, $a \vee b \in I$. 
				\item Let $x \in L$ and $y \in I$. Then there exists $a \in I_1$ and $b \in I_2$ such that $y \leq a \vee b$. Then 
				\begin{align*}
					x \wedge y
					& \leq y \\
					& \leq a \vee b. 
				\end{align*}
				Therefore $x \wedge y \in I$. Since $x \in L$ and $y \in I$ are arbitrary, we have that for each $x \in L$ and $y \in I$, $x \wedge y \in I$. 
			\end{enumerate}
			Therefore $I \in \MI(L)$.
			\item Similar to $(1)$. \tcr{FINISH!!!}
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{proof}
	
	\begin{defn}
		Let $L$ be a lattice. Define ${\leq_{\MI(L)}} \defeq {\subset|_{\MI(L)}}$ \tcr{(maybe standardize notation)}.
	\end{defn}
	
	\begin{ex}
		Let $L$ be a lattice. 
		\begin{enumerate}
			\item Let $I_1, I_2 \in \MI(L)$.
			\begin{enumerate}
				\item $\sup(\{I_1, I_2 \}, \leq_{\MI(L)}) = \{x \in L: \text{there exist $a \in I_1$ and $b \in I_2$ such that $x \leq a \vee b$} \}$.
				\item $\inf(\{I_1, I_2 \}, \leq_{\MI(L)}) = I_1 \cap I_2$
			\end{enumerate}
			\item Set ${\leq_{\MF(L)}} \defeq {\subset|_{\MF(L)}}$ \tcr{(maybe standardize notation)}. Let $F_1, F_2 \in \MF(L)$. \tcr{FIX!!!}
			\begin{enumerate}
				\item $\sup(\{F_1, F_2 \}, \leq_{\MI(L)}) = \{x \in L: \text{there exist $a \in F_1$ and $b \in F_2$ such that $x \leq a \vee b$} \}$.
				\item $\inf(\{F_1, F_2 \}, \leq_{\MF(L)}) = F_1 \cap F_2$
			\end{enumerate}
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{ex}
	
	\begin{proof}\
		\begin{enumerate}
			\item 
			\begin{enumerate}
				\item Set $I \defeq \{x \in L: \text{there exist $a \in I_1$ and $b \in I_2$ such that $x \leq a \vee b$} \}$. \tcr{(ref previous ex)} implies that $I \in \MI(L)$.
				\begin{enumerate}
					\item 
					\begin{itemize}
						\item Let $a \in I_1$. Since $I_2 \in \MI(L)$, $I_2 \neq \varnothing$. Hence there exists $b \in I_2$. Then $a \leq a \vee b$. Since $a \in I_1$, $b \in I_2$ and $a \leq a \vee b$, we have that $a \in I$. Since $a \in I_1$ is arbitrary, we have that for each $a \in I_1$, $a \in I$. Thus $I_1 \subset I$. 
						\item Similarly, $I_2 \subset I$.
					\end{itemize}
					Since $I_1,I_2 \subset I$, we have that $I \in \ubd(\{I_1, I_2\}, \leq_{\MI(L)})$. 
					\item Let $K \in \ubd(\{I_1, I_2\}, \leq_{\MI(L)})$ and $x \in I$. Then $I_1, I_2 \subset K$ and there exist $a \in I_1$ and $b \in I_2$ such that $x \leq a \vee b$. Since $a \in I_1$, $b \in I_2$ and $I_1, I_2 \subset K$, we have that $a,b \in K$. Since $K \in \MI(L)$, $a \vee b \in K$. Since $K \in \MI(L)$ \tcr{(ref a prev ex)} implies that $K$ is a lower set. Since $K$ is a lower set, $a \vee b \in K$ and $x \leq a \vee b$, we have that $x \in K$. Since $x \in I$ is arbitrary, we have that for each $x \in I$, $x \in K$. Hence $I \subset K$. Since $K \in \ubd(\{I_1, I_2\}, \leq_{\MI(L)})$ is arbitrary, we have that for each $K \in \ubd(\{I_1, I_2\}, \leq_{\MI(L)})$, $I \leq_{\MI(L)} K$.
				\end{enumerate}
				Since 
				\begin{enumerate}
					\item $I \in \ubd(\{I_1, I_2\}, \leq_{\MI(L)})$, 
					\item for each $K \in \ubd(\{I_1, I_2\}, \leq_{\MI(L)})$, $I \leq_{\MI(L)} K$,
				\end{enumerate}
				we have that $I = \sup(\{I_1, I_2\}, \leq_{\MI(L)})$.
				\item \tcr{(ref previous ex)} implies that $I_1 \cap I_2 \in \MI(L)$. Since $I_1 \cap I_2 \subset I_1$ and $I_1 \cap I_2 \subset I_2$, we have that $I_1 \cap I_2 \in \lbd(\{I_1, I_2\}, \leq_{\MI(L)})$. Since $(\MI(L), \leq_{\MI(L)})$ is a subposet of $(\MP(L), \subset)$, \tcr{(define subproset/subposet and make exercise for the following fact)} implies that
				\begin{align*}
					\inf (\{I_1, I_2\}, \leq_{\MI(L)})
					& \leq \inf (\{I_1, I_2\}, \subset) \\
					& = I_1 \cap I_2 \\
					& \leq \inf (\{I_1, I_2\}, \leq_{\MI(L)}).
				\end{align*} 
				Hence $I_1 \cap I_2 = \inf (\{I_1, I_2\}, \leq_{\MI(L)})$. 
			\end{enumerate}
			\item Similar to $(1)$. \tcr{FINISH!!!} 
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{proof}
	
	\begin{defn}
		Let $L$ be a lattice. Define $\vee_{\MI(L)}, \wedge_{\MI(L)}: \MI(L) \times \MI(L) \rightarrow \MI(L)$ and $\vee_{\MF(L)}, \wedge_{\MF(L)}: \MF(L) \times \MF(L) \rightarrow \MF(L)$ by 
		\begin{enumerate}
			\item 
			\begin{itemize}
				\item $I_1 \vee_{\MI(L)} I_2 \defeq \{x \in L: \text{there exist $a \in I_1$ and $b \in I_2$ such that $x \leq a \vee b$} \}$
				\item $I_1 \wedge_{\MI(L)} I_2 \defeq I_1 \cap I_2$. 
			\end{itemize}
			\item \tcr{FIX!!!}
			\begin{itemize}
				\item $F_1 \vee_{\MF(L)} F_2 \defeq \{x \in L: \text{there exist $a \in F_1$ and $b \in F_2$ such that $x \leq a \vee b$} \}$
				\item $F_1 \wedge_{\MF(L)} F_2 \defeq F_1 \cap F_2$. 
			\end{itemize}
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{defn}
	
	\begin{ex}
		Let $L$ be a lattice. Then 
		\begin{enumerate}
			\item $(\MI(L), \vee_{\MI(L)}, \wedge_{\MI(L)})$ is a lattice 
			\item $(\MF(L), \vee_{\MF(L)}, \wedge_{\MF(L)})$ is a lattice 
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{ex}
	
	\begin{proof}\
		\begin{enumerate}
			\item \tcr{the previous exercise} implies that $(\MI(L), \leq_{\MI(L)})$ is an ordered lattice. \rex{ex:lattices:lattices:0017} then implies that $(\MI(L), \vee_{\MI(L)}, \wedge_{\MI(L)})$ is a lattice. 
			\item 
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{proof}
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	\subsection{Properties of Ideals and Filters}
	
	
	\begin{defn}
		Let $L$ be a lattice.
		\begin{itemize}
			\item Let $I \in \MI(L)$. Then $I$ is said to be 
			\begin{itemize}
				\item \tbf{proper} if $I \neq L$. 
				\item \tbf{maximal} if $I$ is maximal in $(\MI(L) \setminus \{L\}, \subset)$. 
				\item \tbf{prime} if 
				\begin{enumerate}
					\item $I$ is proper 
					\item for each $a,b \in L$, $a \wedge b \in I$ implies that $a \in I$ or $b \in I$. 
				\end{enumerate}
				\item \tbf{principal} if there exists $a \in L$ such that $I = \doar a$.
			\end{itemize}
			\item Let $F \in \MF(L)$. Then $F$ is said to be 
			\begin{itemize}
				\item \tbf{proper} if $F \neq L$. 
				\item \tbf{maximal} if $F$ is maximal in $(\MF(L) \setminus \{L\}, \subset)$. 
				\item \tbf{prime} if 
				\begin{enumerate}
					\item $F$ is proper 
					\item for each $a,b \in L$, $a \vee b \in F$ implies that $a \in F$ or $b \in F$.
				\end{enumerate}
				\item \tbf{principal} if there exists $a \in L$ such that $F = \upar a$. 
			\end{itemize}
		\end{itemize}
	\end{defn}
	
	\begin{ex}
		Let $L$ be a lattice.
		\begin{enumerate}
			\item For each $I \in \MI(L)$. 
			\begin{enumerate}
				\item if $I$ is proper, then for each $x \in I$, $x$ is not a one of $L$.
				\item if $I$ is prinicpal, then $I$ is proper iff for each $x \in I$, $x$ is not a one of $L$.
			\end{enumerate}
			\item For each $F \in \MF(L)$. 
			\begin{enumerate}
				\item If $F$ is proper, then for each $x \in F$, $x$ is not a zero of $L$.
				\item If $F$ is prinicpal, then $F$ is proper iff for each $x \in F$, $x$ is not a zero of $L$.
			\end{enumerate}
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{ex}
	
	\begin{proof}\
		\begin{enumerate}
			\item Let $I \in \MI(L)$.
			\begin{enumerate}
				\item Suppose that $I$ is proper. Let $x \in I$. For the sake of contradiction, suppose that $x$ is a one of $L$. \tcr{(previous ex)} then implies that $\ub(L, \leq) = \{x\}$. Let $y \in L$. Then $y \leq x$. Since $I$ is a lower set, $x \in I$ and $y \leq x$, we have that $y \in I$. Since $y \in L$ is arbitrary, we have that for each $y \in L$, $y \in I$. Thus 
				\begin{align*}
					L 
					& \subset I \\
					& \subset L.
				\end{align*}
				Thus $I = L$ and $I$ is not proper. Therefore $x$ is not a one of $L$. Since $x \in I$ is arbitrary, we have that for each $x \in I$, $x$ is not a one of $L$. 
				\item Suppose that $I$ is principal. 
				\begin{itemize}
					\item $(\implies)$: \\
					If $I$ is proper, then $1(a)$ implies that for each $x \in I$, $x$ is not a one of $L$.
					\item $(\impliedby)$: \\
					Suppose that $I$ is not proper. Then $I = L$. Since $I$ is principal, there exists $a \in I$ such that $I = \doar a$. Let $x \in L$. Since
					\begin{align*}
						x
						& \in L \\
						& = I \\
						& = \doar a,
					\end{align*} 
					we have that $x \leq a$. Hence 
					\begin{align*}
						x \wedge a
						& = \inf \{x, a\} \\
						& = x.
					\end{align*}
					Since $x \in L$ is arbitrary, we have that for each $x \in L$, $x \wedge a = x$. Hence $a$ is a one of $L$. Therefore $I$ is not proper implies that there exists $a \in I$ such that $a$ is a one of $L$. By contrapositive, we have that if for each $x \in I$, $x$ is not a one of $L$, then $I$ is proper. 
				\end{itemize}
			\end{enumerate}
			\item \tcr{FINISH!!}
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{proof}
	
	\begin{ex}
		Let $L$ be a lattice and $x \in L$.
		\begin{enumerate}
			\item Then $\doar x \in \MI_p(L)$ iff $x \in \prmm(L)$.
			\item Then $\upar x \in \MF_p(L)$ iff $x \in \prmj(L)$.
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{ex}
	
	\begin{proof}\
		\begin{enumerate}
			\item 
			\begin{itemize}
				\item $(\implies)$: \\
				Suppose that $\doar x \in \MI_p(L)$. Then $\doar x$ is proper and for each $a,b \in L$, $a \wedge b \in \doar x$ implies that $a \in \doar x$ or $b \in \doar x$. 
				\begin{itemize}
					\item Since $\doar x$ is proper, \tcr{previous exercise} implies that $x$ is not a one of $L$. 
					\item Let $a,b \in L$. Suppose that $a \wedge b \leq x$. Since $\doar x$ is a lower set, $x \in \doar x$ and $a \wedge b \leq x$, we have that $a \wedge b \in \doar x$. Therefore $a \in \doar x$ or $b \in \doar x$. Then $a \leq x$ or $b \leq x$. Since $a,b \in L$ with $a \wedge b \leq x$ are arbitrary, we have that for each $a, b \in L$, $a \wedge b \leq x$ implies that $a \leq x$ or $b \leq x$. 
				\end{itemize}
				Thus $x \in \prmm(L)$.
				\item $(\impliedby)$: \\
				Suppose that $x \in \prmm(L)$.
				\begin{itemize}
					\item For the sake of contradiction, suppose that $\doar x$ is not proper. Then $\doar x = L$. Thus $\ubd L = \{x\}$. \tcr{A previous ex} implies that $x$ is a one of $L$. This is a contracition since $x \in \prmm(L)$. Therefore $\doar x$ is proper. 
					\item Let $a,b \in L$. Suppose that $a \wedge b \in \doar x$. Then $a \wedge b \leq x$. Since $x \in \prmm(L)$, $a \leq x$ or $b \leq x$. Hence $a \in \doar x$ or $b \in \doar x$. Since $a,b \in L$ with $a \wedge b \in \doar x$ are arbitrary, we have that for each $a,b \in L$, $a \wedge b \in \doar x$ implies that $a \in \doar x$ or $b \in \doar x$.
				\end{itemize}
				Therefore $\doar x \in \MI_p(L)$.
			\end{itemize}
			\item Similar to $(1)$ \tcr{FIX/CHECK/FINISH!!!}
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{proof}
	
	\begin{ex}
		Let $L$ be a lattice.
		\begin{enumerate}
			\item For each $I \in \MI(L)$, $I \in \MI_p(L)$ iff $I^c \in \MF(L)$.
			\item For each $F \in \MF(L)$, $F \in \MF_p(L)$ iff $F^c \in \MI(L)$.
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{ex}
	
	\begin{proof}\
		\begin{enumerate}
			\item Let $I \in \MI(L)$. 
			\begin{itemize}
				\item $(\implies)$: \\
				Suppose that $I \in \MI_p(L)$. Then $I$ is proper and for each $a,b \in L$, $a \wedge b \in I$ implies that $a \in I$ or $b \in I$. 
				\begin{enumerate}
					\item Since $I$ is proper, $I \neq L$. Therefore $I^c \neq \varnothing$
					\item Let $a,b \in I^c$. For the sake of contradiction, suppose that $a \wedge b \in I$. Since $I \in \MI_p(L)$, $a \in I$ or $b \in I$. This is a contradiction since $a \in I^c$ and $b \in I^c$. Therefore $a \wedge b \not \in I$. Hence $a \wedge b \in I^c$. Since $a,b \in I^c$ are arbitrary, we have that for each for each  $a,b \in I^c$, $a \wedge b \in I^c$. 
					\item Since $I \in \MI(L)$, \tcr{a previous exercise} implies that $I$ is a lower set. \tcr{an exercise in proset section} implies that $I^c$ is an upper set. Let $x \in L$ and $a \in I^c$. Since 
					\begin{align*}
						x \vee a
						& \geq a \\
						& \in I^c
					\end{align*}
					and $I^c$ is an upper set, we have that $x \vee a \in I^c$. Since $x \in L$ and $a \in I^c$ are arbitrary, we have that for each for each $x \in L$ and $a \in I^c$, $x \vee a \in I^c$.1
				\end{enumerate}
				Thus $I^c \in \MF(L)$. 
				\item $(\impliedby)$: \\
				Suppose that $I^c \in \MF(L)$.
				\begin{enumerate}
					\item Since $I^c \in \MF(L)$, $I^c \neq \varnothing$. Then $I \neq L$ and therefore $I$ is proper.  
					\item Let $a,b \in L$. Suppose that $a \wedge b \in I$. For the sake of contradiction, suppose that $a \not \in I$ and $b \not \in I$. Then $a \in I^c$ and $b \in I^c$. Since $I^c \in \MF(L)$, we have that $a \wedge b \in I^c$. This is a contradiction since $a \wedge b \in I$. Hence $a \in I$ or $b \in I$. Since $a, b \in L$ with $a \wedge b \in I$ are arbitrary, we have that for each $a, b \in L$, $a \wedge b \in I$ implies that $a \in I$ or $b \in I$.
				\end{enumerate}
				Thus $I \in \MI_p(L)$. 
			\end{itemize}
			\item \tcr{use duality}.
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{proof}
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	\section{Contructions with Lattices}
	
	\subsection{Sublattices}
	
	\begin{defn}
		Let $(L, \vee_L, \wedge_L)$ and $(M, \vee_M, \wedge_M)$ be lattices. Then $(M, \vee_M, \wedge_M)$ is said to be a \tbf{sublattice of $(L, \vee_L, \wedge_L)$} if
		\begin{enumerate}
			\item $M \subset L$
			\item $\iota_M$ is a $((\vee_L, \wedge_L), (\vee_M, \wedge_M))$-homomorphism.
		\end{enumerate}  
	\end{defn}
	
	\begin{ex}
		Let $(L, \vee_L, \wedge_L)$ and $(M, \vee_M, \wedge_M)$ be lattices. Then $(M, \vee_M, \wedge_M)$ is a sublattice of $L$ iff
		\begin{enumerate}
			\item $M \subset L$
			\item $\vee_L|_{M^2} = \vee_M$ and $\wedge_L|_{M^2} = \wedge_M$. 
		\end{enumerate}  
	\end{ex}
	
	\begin{proof}\
		\begin{itemize}
			\item $(\implies)$: \\
			Suppose that $(M, \vee_M, \wedge_M)$ is a sublattice of $L$. 
			\begin{enumerate}
				\item By definition, $M \subset L$.
				\item By definiton, $\iota_M$ is a $((\vee_L, \wedge_L), (\vee_M, \wedge_M))$-homomorphism. Then for each $a,b \in M$, 
				\begin{align*}
					\vee_M(a,b)
					& = a \vee_M b \\
					& = \iota_M(a \vee_M b) \\
					& = \iota_M(a) \vee_L \iota_M(b) \\
					& = a \vee_L b \\
					& = \vee_L|_{M^2}(a,b). 
				\end{align*} 
				Hence $\vee_L|_{M^2} = \vee_M$. Similarly, $\wedge_L|_{M^2} = \wedge_M$. 
			\end{enumerate}
			\item  $(\impliedby)$: \\
			Suppose that 
			\begin{enumerate}
				\item $M \subset L$
				\item $\vee_L|_{M^2} = \vee_M$ and $\wedge_L|_{M^2} = \wedge_M$. 
			\end{enumerate} 
			\begin{enumerate}
				\item By assumption $M \subset L$. 
				\item Since $\vee_L|_{M^2} = \vee_M$, we have that for each $a,b \in M$, 
				\begin{align*}
					\iota_M(a \vee_M b)
					& = a \vee_M b \\
					& = \vee_M(a,b) \\
					& = \vee_L|_{M^2}(a,b) \\
					& = \vee_L(a,b) \\
					& = a \vee_L b.
				\end{align*}
				Hence $\iota_M$ is a $(\vee_M, \vee_L)$-homomorphism. Similarly, $\iota_M$ is a $(\wedge_M, \wedge_L)$-homomorphism. Therefore $\iota_M$ is a $((\vee_L, \wedge_L), (\vee_M, \wedge_M))$-homomorphism. 
			\end{enumerate}
			Thus $(M, \vee_M, \wedge_M)$ is a sublattice of $L$. 
		\end{itemize}
		\tcr{FINISH!!!!}
	\end{proof}
	
	\tcr{need to make preliminary ex showing that if $M \subset L$, $M \neq \varnothing$ and $\vee(M^2), \wedge(M ^2) \subset M$, then $(M, \vee|_{M^2}, \wedge|_{M^2})$} is a lattice

	\begin{ex}
		Let $(L, \vee, \wedge)$ be a lattice and $M \subset L$. Then $(M, \vee|_{M^2}, \wedge|_{M^2})$ is a sublattice of $(L, \vee, \wedge)$ iff
		\begin{enumerate}
			\item $M \neq \varnothing$
			\item $\vee(M^2), \wedge(M^2) \subset M$.
		\end{enumerate}
 	\end{ex}
 	
 	\begin{proof}
 		Set $\vee_M \defeq \vee|_{M^2}$ and $\wedge_M \defeq \wedge|_{M^2}$ 
 		\begin{itemize}
 			\item $(\implies)$: \\
 			Suppose that $(M, \vee_M, \wedge_M)$ is a sublattice of $(L, \vee, \wedge)$. Since $(M, \vee_M, \wedge_M)$ is a sublattice of $(L, \vee, \wedge)$, we have that $(M, \vee_M, \wedge_M)$ is a lattice.
 			\begin{enumerate}
 				\item Since $(M, \vee_M, \wedge_M)$ is a lattice, $M \neq \varnothing$.
 				\item Since $(M, \vee_M, \wedge_M)$ is a lattice, $\vee_M(M^2), \wedge_M(M^2) \subset M$. 
 			\end{enumerate}
 			\item $(\impliedby)$: \\ 
 			Suppose that 
 			\begin{enumerate}
 				\item $M \neq \varnothing$
 				\item $\vee(M^2), \wedge(M^2) \subset M$.
 			\end{enumerate}
 			\begin{enumerate}
 				\item By assumption $M \neq \varnothing$,
 				\item Since $\vee, \wedge$ are semilattice operators, $\vee, \wedge$ satisfy the lattice absorption identities and $\vee(M^2), \wedge(M^2) \subset M$, we have that $\vee_M, \wedge_M$ are semilattice operators and $\vee|_M, \wedge|_M$ satisfy the lattice absorption identities. 
 			\end{enumerate}
 			Thus $(M, \vee_M, \wedge_M)$ is a lattice. By assumption, $M \subset L$ and $\vee_M = \vee|_{M^2}$, $\wedge_M = \wedge|_{M^2}$, we have that $(M, \vee_M, \wedge_M)$ is a sublattice of $(L, \vee, \wedge)$.
 		\end{itemize}
 	\end{proof}

	\section{Introduction}
	
	\begin{ex}
		Let $(X, \leq)$ be a poset. Then for each $A \subset X$, $\sup A$ exists iff for each $A \subset X$, $\inf A$ exists. 
	\end{ex}
	
	\begin{proof}\
		\begin{itemize}
			\item $(\implies)$: \\
			Suppose that for each $A \subset X$, $\sup A$ exists. Let $A \subset X$. Since $\lbd A \subset X$, by assumption, $\sup (\lbd A)$ exists. Since
			\begin{align*}
				\text{$\sup (\lbd A)$ exists}
				& \iff \sup (\lbd A) \neq \varnothing \quad \text{(by \tcr{ref def here})}\\
				& \iff \inf A \neq \varnothing \quad \text{(by \rex{ex:orderings:posets:0006.1})} \\
				& \iff \text{$\inf A$ exists},
			\end{align*}
			we have that $\inf A$ exists.
			\item $(\impliedby)$: \\
			Similar to $(\implies)$.
		\end{itemize}
	\end{proof}
	
	\begin{defn}
		Let $(X, \leq)$ be a poset. 
		\begin{itemize}
			\item Then $(X, \leq)$ is said to be a \tbf{complete lattice} if for each $A \subset X$, $\sup A$ exists. 
			\item If $(X, \leq)$ is a complete lattice, we define the \tbf{arbitrary join and meet operators on $X$}, denoted $\bigvee_{\leq} : \MP(X) \rightarrow X$ and $\bigwedge_{\leq} : \MP(X) \rightarrow X$ respectively, by $\bigvee_{\leq} A \defeq \sup (A, \leq)$ and $\bigwedge_{\leq} A \defeq \inf (A, \leq)$. 
		\end{itemize}
	\end{defn}
	
	\begin{note}
		When the context is clear, we may write $\bigvee$ and $\bigwedge$ in place of $\bigvee_{\leq}$ and $\bigwedge_{\leq}$ respectively.
	\end{note}
	
	\begin{ex}
		Let $(X, \leq)$ be a complete lattice. Then $(X, \vee_{\leq}, \wedge_{\leq}, \inf(L, \leq), \sup(L, \leq),)$ is a bounded lattice.  
	\end{ex}
	
	\begin{proof}\
		\tcr{FIX!!!}
		\begin{enumerate}
			\item \tcr{make previous exercise} implies that $\lbd(\varnothing, \leq) = X$. Since $(X, \leq)$ is a complete lattice and $\varnothing \subset X$, there exists $x_1 \in X$ such that $x_1 \defeq \inf \varnothing$. In particular, $X \neq \varnothing$.
			\item 
			\begin{itemize}
				\item \tcr{make previous ex saying $\inf \varnothing = \sup X$ and $\sup \varnothing = \inf X$ with the following logic} 
				\begin{align*}
					\inf \varnothing \\
					& \in \lbd \varnothing \cap \ubd (\lbd \varnothing) \\
					& = X \cap \lbd (X) \\
					& = \lbd X. 
				\end{align*}
				\tcr{ref an ex} implying that $\lbd X = \inf X$. Hence $X$ is a one of $X$. 
				\item similar $x_0$
			\end{itemize}
		\end{enumerate}
		Clearly $\bigvee X$ is a one of $X$ and $\bigwedge X$ is a zero of $X$.
	\end{proof}
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	\section{Complete Lattice Homomorphisms}
	
	\begin{defn}
		Let $(X, \leq_X), (Y, \leq_Y)$ be complete lattices and $f:X \rightarrow Y$. Then $f$ is said to 
		\begin{enumerate}
			\item \tbf{$(\leq_X, \leq_Y)$-arbitrary join preserving} if for each $A \subset X$, $f(\sup (A, \leq_X) ) = \sup(f(A), \leq_Y)$
			\item \tbf{$(\leq_X, \leq_Y)$-arbitrary meet preserving} if for each $A \subset X$, $f(\inf (A, \leq_X) ) = \inf(f(A), \leq_Y)$
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{defn}
	
	\begin{ex}
		Let $(X, \leq_X), (Y, \leq_Y)$ be complete lattices and $f:X \rightarrow Y$. Then 
		\begin{enumerate}
			\item $f$ is $(\leq_X, \leq_Y)$-arbitrary join preserving iff $f$ is $(\lop_X, \lop_Y)$-arbitrary meet preserving.
			\item $f$ is $(\leq_X, \leq_Y)$-arbitrary meet preserving iff $f$ is $(\lop_X, \lop_Y)$-arbitrary join preserving.
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{ex}
	
	\begin{proof}\
		\begin{enumerate}
			\item 
			\begin{itemize}
				\item $(\implies)$: \\
				Suppose that $f$ is $(\leq_X, \leq_Y)$-arbitrary join preserving. Then \tcr{a prev ex in poset section on suprema} implies that for each $A \subset X$, 
				\begin{align}
					f(\inf(A, \lop_X))
					& = f(\sup (A, \leq_X)) \\
					& = \sup(f(A), \leq_Y) \\
					& = \inf(f(A), \lop_Y).
				\end{align}
				Hence $f$ is $(\lop_X, \lop_Y)$-arbitrary meet preserving.
				\item $(\impliedby)$: \\
				Similar to $(\implies)$. \tcr{ref}.
			\end{itemize}
			\item Similar to $(1)$. \tcr{ref}.
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{proof}
	
	
	\begin{ex}
		Let $(X, \leq_X), (Y, \leq_Y)$ be complete lattices.
		\begin{enumerate}
			\item For each $f:X \rightarrow Y$, $f$ is arbitrary join preserving iff $f$ has a right adjoint. 
			\item For each $g:Y \rightarrow X$, $g$ is arbitrary meet preserving iff $g$ has a left adjoint. 
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{ex}
	
	\begin{proof}\
		\begin{enumerate}
			\item 
			\begin{itemize}
				\item $(\implies)$: \\
				Let $f:X \rightarrow Y$. Suppose that $f$ is arbitrary join preserving. \tcr{an ex in subsection on semilattice homomorphisms} \rex{} implies that $f$ is monotone. Let $b \in Y$. \rex{ex:orderings:posets:0008} implies that $\sup \doar b = b$. Define $a \in X$ by $a \defeq \sup f^{-1}(\doar b)$. 
				\begin{itemize}
					\item Let $x \in f^{-1}(\doar b)$. Then 
					\begin{align*}
						x 
						& \leq \sup f^{-1}(\doar b) \\
						& = a.
					\end{align*}
					Thus $x \in \doar a$. Since $x \in f^{-1}(\doar b)$ is arbitrary, we have that for each $x \in f^{-1}(\doar b)$, $x \in \doar a$. Hence $f^{-1}(\doar b) \subset \doar a$. 
					\item We note that for each $x \in X$,
					\begin{align*}
						x \in \doar a
						& \iff x \leq_X a \\
						& \implies f(x) \leq_Y f(a).
					\end{align*}
					Let $x \in \doar a$. \tcr{An exercise in basic set theory ex} implies that $f(f^{-1}(\doar b)) \subset \doar b$. \rex{ex:orderings:posets:0006.2} then implies that 
					\begin{align*}
						f(x) 
						& \leq_Y f(a) \\
						& = f(\sup f^{-1}(\doar b) ) \\
						& = \sup f (f^{-1}(\doar b)) \\
						& \leq \sup \doar b \\
						& = b.
					\end{align*}
					Then $f(x) \in \doar b$. Hence $x \in f^{-1}(\doar b)$. Since $x \in \doar a$ is arbitrary, we have that for each $x \in \doar a$, $x \in f^{-1}(\doar b)$. Hence $\doar a \subset f^{-1}(\doar b)$. 
				\end{itemize}  
				Since $f^{-1}(\doar b) \subset \doar a$ and $\doar a \subset f^{-1}(\doar b)$, we have that $f^{-1}(\doar b) = \doar a$. Since $b \in Y$ is arbitrary, we have that for each $b \in Y$, there exists $a \in X$ such that $f^{-1}(\doar b) = \doar a$. \tcr{A prev ex in proset section on galois connections} \rex{} then implies that $f$ has a right adjoint.
				\item $(\impliedby)$: \\
				Suppose that $f$ has a right adjoint. Then there exists $f_*: Y \rightarrow X$ such that $(f, f_*)$ is a monotone Galois connection. \rex{ex:orderings:posets:0010} implies that for each $A \subset X$, $f(\sup A) = \sup f(A)$. Hence $f$ is arbitrary join preserving.
			\end{itemize}
			\item Let $g:Y \rightarrow X$. \tcr{the prev part, the prev ex and an ex in galois connection poset subsection} imply that
			\begin{align*}
				\text{$g$ is $(\leq_Y, \leq_X)$-arbitrary meet preserving}
				& \iff \text{$g$ is $(\lop_Y, \lop_X)$-arbitrary join preserving} \\
				& \iff \text{$g$ has a $(\lop_Y, \lop_X)$-right adjoint} \\
				& \iff \text{there exists $g^*: X \rightarrow Y$ such that} \\
				& \quad \quad \text{$(g, g^*)$ is a $(\lop_Y, \lop_X)$-monotone Galois connection} \\
				& \iff \text{there exists $g^*: X \rightarrow Y$ such that} \\
				& \quad \quad \text{$(g^*, g)$ is a $(\leq_X, \leq_Y)$-monotone Galois connection} \\
				& \iff \text{$g$ has a $(\leq_X, \leq_Y)$-left adjoint}.
			\end{align*}
			Since $g:Y \rightarrow X$ is arbitrary, we have that for each $g:Y \rightarrow X$, $g$ is $(\leq_Y, \leq_X)$-arbitrary meet preserving iff there exists $g^*: Y \rightarrow X$ such that $g^*$ is a $(\leq_X, \leq_Y)$-left adjoint of $g$.
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{proof}
	
	\begin{ex}
		Let $(X, \leq_X), (Y, \leq_Y)$ be complete lattices, $f:X \rightarrow Y$ and $g: Y \rightarrow X$. Suppose that $(f,g)$ is a monotone Galois connection. 
		\begin{enumerate}
			\item 
			\begin{enumerate}
				\item 
				\item 
			\end{enumerate}
			\item 
			\begin{enumerate}
				\item $g(1_Y) = 1_X$ iff
				\item 
			\end{enumerate}
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{ex}
	
	\begin{ex}
		show that if $g$ is a right adjoint, then $g(\inf \upar x) = \inf g(\upar x) = \inf \upar(g(x)) = g(x)$
	\end{ex}

	\section{Properties of Ideals and Filters}
	
	\subsection{Prime Ideals and Filters in Complete Lattices}
	
	\begin{defn}
		Let $L$ be a complete lattice. 
		\begin{itemize}
			\item Let $I \in \MI(L)$. Then $I$ is said to be
			\begin{itemize}
				\item \tbf{completely prime} if
				\begin{enumerate}
					\item $I$ is proper
					\item for each $A \subset L$, $\bigwedge A \in I$ implies that there exists $a \in A$ such that $a \in I$.
				\end{enumerate}
			\end{itemize} 
			\item Let $F \in \MF(L)$. Then $F$ is said to be 
			\begin{itemize}
				\item \tbf{completely prime} if 
				\begin{enumerate}
					\item $F$ is proper
					\item for each $A \subset L$, $\bigvee A \in F$ implies that there exists $a \in A$ such that $a \in F$.
				\end{enumerate}
			\end{itemize} 
		\end{itemize}
	\end{defn}
	
	\begin{ex}
		Let $L$ be a complete lattice.
		\begin{enumerate}
			\item For each $I \in \MI(L)$, if $I$ is completely prime, then $I \in \MI_p(L)$.
			\item For each $F \in \MF(L)$, if $F$ is completely prime, then $F \in \MF_p(L)$. 
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{ex}
	
	\begin{proof}
		Clear. \tcr{fill out}
	\end{proof}
	
	\begin{ex}
		Let $L$ be a complete lattice.
		\begin{enumerate}
			\item For each $I \in \MI_{cp}(L)$,
			\begin{enumerate}
				\item $\bigwedge I^c \in I^c$,
				\item $\bigwedge I^c \in \prmj(L)$.
			\end{enumerate}
			\item For each $F \in \MF_{cp}(L)$,
			\begin{enumerate}
				\item $\bigvee F^c \in F^c$,
				\item $\bigvee F^c \in \prmm(L)$.
			\end{enumerate}
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{ex}
	
	\begin{proof}\
		\begin{enumerate}
			\item Let $I \in \MI_{cp}(L)$. 
			\begin{enumerate}
				\item For the sake of contradiction, suppose that $\bigwedge I^c \in I$. Since $I$ is completely prime, there exists $a \in I^c$ such that $a \in I$. This is a contradiction. Hence $\bigwedge I^c \not \in I$. Thus $\bigwedge I^c \in I^c$. 
				\item 
				\begin{enumerate}
					\item Since $I \in \MI(L)$, \tcr{a previous ex} implies that $0 \in I$. Since $\bigwedge I^c \in I^c$, we have that $\bigwedge I^c \neq 0$. 
					\item Define $x \in L$ by $x \defeq \bigwedge I^c$. Since $I \in \MI(L)$, \tcr{a prev ex} implies that $I$ is a lower set. Let $a,b \in L$. Suppose that $x \leq a \vee b$. For the sake of contradiction, suppose that $a \in I$ and $b \in I$. Since $I \in \MI(L)$, we have that $a \vee b \in I$. Since $I$ is a lower set and 
					\begin{align*}
						x
						& \leq a \vee b \\
						& \in I,
					\end{align*}
					we have that $x \in I$. This is a contradiction since $x \in I^c$. Hence $a \not \in I$ or $b \not \in I$. Thus $a \in I^c$ or $b \in I^c$. Since $x = \bigwedge I^c$, we have that $x \leq a$ or $x \leq b$. Since $a,b \in L$ with $x \leq a \vee b$ are arbitrary, we have that for each $a,b \in L$, $x \leq a \vee b$ implies that $x \leq a$ or $b \leq b$.
				\end{enumerate}
				 Hence $x \in \prmj(L)$.
			\end{enumerate} 
			\item \tcr{use duality}
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{proof}
	
	\begin{ex}
		Let $L$ be a complete lattice. Then 
		\begin{enumerate}
			\item for each $x \in \prmj(L)$, $(\upar x)^c \in \MI_{cp}(L)$.
			\item for each $x \in \prmm(L)$, $(\doar x)^c \in \MF_{cp}(L)$.
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{ex}
	
	\begin{proof}\
		\begin{enumerate}
			\item Let $x \in \prmj(L)$. \tcr{a prev ex} implies that $\upar x \in \MF(L)$. 
			\begin{enumerate}
				\item Since $x \in \prmj(L)$, $x \neq 0$. Hence $0 \in (\upar x)^c$. Thus $(\upar x)^c \neq \varnothing$.
				\item Let $a,b \in (\upar x)^c$. For the sake of contradiction, suppose that $a \vee b \in \upar x$. Then $x \leq a \vee b$. Since $x \in \prmj(L)$, $x \leq a$ or $x \leq b$. Hence $a \in \upar x$ or $b \in \upar x$. This is a contraction since $a \in (\upar x)^c$ and $b \in (\upar x)^c$. Therefore $a \vee b \in (\upar x)^c$. Since $a,b \in (\upar x)^c$ are arbitrary, we have that for each $a,b \in (\upar x)^c$, $a \vee b \in (\upar x)^c$.
				\item Let $y \in L$ and $a \in (\upar x)^c$. For the sake of contradiction, suppose that $y \wedge a \in \upar x$. Then 
				\begin{align*}
					x 
					& \leq y \wedge a \\
					& \leq a.
				\end{align*}
				Thus $a \in \upar x$, which is a contradiction. Hence $y \wedge a \in (\upar x)^c$. Since $y \in L$ and $a \in (\upar x)^c$ are arbitrary, we have that for each $y \in L$ and $a \in (\upar x)^c$,  $y \wedge a \in (\upar x)^c$. 
			\end{enumerate}
			Thus $(\upar x)^c \in \MI(L)$.
			\begin{enumerate}
				\item Since $\upar x \neq \varnothing$, we have that $(\upar x)^c \neq L$. Thus $(\upar x)^c$ is proper.
				\item Let $A \subset L$. Suppose that $\bigwedge A \in (\upar x)^c$. For the sake of contradiction, suppose that for each $a \in A$, $a \not \in (\upar x)^c$. Then for each $a \in A$, $a \in \upar x$. Hence for each $a \in A$, $x \leq a$. Therefore $x \in \lbd A$. Thus 
				\begin{align*}
					x
					& \leq \inf A \\
					& = \bigwedge A.
				\end{align*}
				Then $\bigwedge A \in \upar x$, which is a contradiction. Hence there exists $a \in A$ such that $a \in (\upar x)^c$. Since $A \subset L$ with $\bigwedge A \in (\upar x)^c$ is arbitrary, we have that for each $A \subset L$, $\bigwedge A \in (\upar x)^c$ implies that there exists $a \in A$ such that $a \in (\upar x)^c$.
			\end{enumerate}
			Thus $(\upar x)^c \in \MI_{cp}(L)$.
			\item \tcr{similar or duality}
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{proof}

	\section{Least Upper Bound Property}

	\begin{defn} 
		Let $(X, \leq)$ be a poset. Then $(X, \leq)$ is said to satisfy the 
		\begin{itemize}
			\item \tbf{least upper bound (LUB) property} if for each $A \subset X$, if $A \neq \varnothing$ and $A$ is bounded above, then there exists $x \in X$ such that $x = \sup A$
			\item \tbf{greatest lower bound (GLB) property} if for each $A \subset X$, if $A \neq \varnothing$ and $A$ is bounded below, then there exists $x \in X$ such that $x = \inf A$. 
		\end{itemize}
	\end{defn}
	
	\begin{ex}
		LUB iff GLB
	\end{ex}
	
	\begin{proof}
		\tcr{FINISH!!!!}
	\end{proof}
	
	\begin{defn} \tbf{Suplattice:} \\
		Let $(L, \leq)$ be a poset. Then $(L, \leq)$ is said to be a \tbf{suplattice} if for each $A \subset L$, there exists $x \in L$ such that $x = \sup A$. 
	\end{defn}

	\newpage
	\chapter{Some Chapter}
	
	\section{Closure Operators}
	
	\begin{defn}
		Let $A$ be a set and $C: \MP(A) \rightarrow \MP(A)$. Then $C$ is said to be a \tbf{closure operator on $A$} if for each $X, Y \in \MP(A)$,
		\begin{enumerate}
			\item $X \subset C(X)$,
			\item $C^2(X) = C(X)$,
			\item $X \subset Y$ implies that $C(X) \subset C(Y)$.
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{defn}

	\begin{ex}
		Let $A$ be a set and $C: \MP(A) \rightarrow \MP(A)$. Suppose that $C$ is a closure operator on $A$. Then for each $(E_j)_{j \in J} \subset \MP(A)$,
		\begin{enumerate}
			\item $C \bigg( \bigcap\limits_{j \in J} E_j \bigg) \subset \bigcap\limits_{k \in J} C(E_k)$,
			\item $\bigcup\limits_{k \in J} C(E_k) \subset C \bigg( \bigcup\limits_{j \in J} E_j \bigg)$.
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{ex}

	\begin{proof}
		Let $(E_j)_{j \in J} \subset \MP(A)$. 
		\begin{enumerate}
			\item Let $k \in J$. Then $\bigcap\limits_{j \in J} E_j \subset E_k$.
			So $C \bigg( \bigcap\limits_{j \in J} E_j \bigg) \subset C(E_k)$. Since $k \in J$ is arbitrary, we have that 
			$$C \bigg( \bigcap\limits_{j \in J} E_j \bigg) \subset \bigcap\limits_{k \in J} C(E_k).$$ 
			\item Let $k \in J$. Then $E_k \subset \bigcup_{j \in J} E_j$. Hence $C(E_k) \subset C \bigg( \bigcup\limits_{j \in J} E_j \bigg)$. Since $k \in J$ is arbitrary, we have that 
			\begin{align*}
				\bigcup\limits_{k \in J} C(E_k) 
				& \subset C \bigg( \bigcup\limits_{j \in J} E_j \bigg)
			\end{align*}
			
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{proof}

	\begin{defn}
		Let $A$ be a set, $C: \MP(A) \rightarrow \MP(A)$ and $X \subset A$. Suppose that $C$ is a closure operator on $A$. Then $X$ is said to be $C$-closed if $C(X) = X$.  
	\end{defn}

	\begin{defn}
		Let $A$ be a set and $C: \MP(A) \rightarrow \MP(A)$. Suppose that $C$ is a closure operator on $A$. We define the \tbf{lattice of $C$-closed subsets of $A$}, denoted $L_C(A) \subset \MP(A)$, by 
		$$L_C(A) \defeq \{X \subset A: \text{$X$ is $C$-closed}\}$$. 
	\end{defn}


	\begin{ex}
		Let $A$ be a set and $C: \MP(A) \rightarrow \MP(A)$. Suppose that $C$ is a closure operator on $A$. Then
		\begin{enumerate}
			\item for each $(E_j)_{j \in J} \subset L_C(A)$, $\bigcap\limits_{j \in J} E_j \in L_C(A)$ and $\bigcup\limits_{j \in J} E_j \in L_C(A)$.
			\item $(L_C(A), \subset)$ is a complete lattice
			\tcr{define complete lattice}
			$$ C \bigg( \bigcap\limits_{j \in J} E_j \bigg) = \bigcap\limits_{j \in J} E_j $$ 
			and  
			$$ C \bigg(  \bigcup\limits_{j \in J} E_j \bigg) = \bigcup\limits_{j \in J} E_j.$$
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{ex}

	\begin{proof}\
		\begin{enumerate}
			\item Let $(E_j)_{j \in J} \subset L_C(A)$. 
			\begin{itemize}
				\item \tcr{A previous exercise} \rex{} implies that
				\begin{align*}
					C \bigg( \bigcap\limits_{j \in J} E_j \bigg) 
					& \subset \bigcap\limits_{k \in J} C(E_k) \\
					& = \bigcap\limits_{k \in J} E_k \\
					& \subset C \bigg( \bigcap\limits_{k \in J} E_k \bigg). 
				\end{align*}
				Hence $C \bigg( \bigcap\limits_{j \in J} E_j \bigg) = \bigcap\limits_{k \in J} E_k$.
				\item \tcr{A previous exercise} \rex{} implies that
				\begin{align*}
					\bigcup\limits_{k \in J} E_k 
					& = \bigcup\limits_{k \in J} C(E_k) \\
					& \subset C \bigg( \bigcup\limits_{j \in J} E_j \bigg) \\
					& \subset \bigcap\limits_{k \in J} C(E_k) \\
					& = \bigcap\limits_{k \in J} E_k \\
					& \subset C \bigg( \bigcap\limits_{k \in J} E_k \bigg). 
				\end{align*}
				Hence $C \bigg( \bigcap\limits_{j \in J} E_j \bigg) = \bigcap\limits_{k \in J} E_k$.
			\end{itemize}
			\item 
		\end{enumerate}
		\tcr{FINISH!!!, don't need to show second part, }
	\end{proof}

	\begin{defn}
		then is said to be an \tbf{algebraic closure operator on $A$} if 
	\end{defn}

	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	\newpage
	\chapter{Universal Algebra}
	
	\section{Introduction}
	
	\begin{defn}
		Let $A,J \in \Obj(\Set)$ be a set and $f \in \MF^*(A)^J$. Then $(A, f)$ is said to be an \tbf{algebra} if $A \neq \varnothing$ and $J \neq \varnothing$. 
	\end{defn}
	
	\begin{defn}
		Let $\MA$ be an algebra. Write $\MA = (A, f)$. Set $J \defeq \dom f$. 
		\begin{itemize}
			\item We define the \tbf{universe of $\MA$}, denoted $\uni\MA$, by $\uni\MA \defeq A$.
			\item We define the \tbf{operations of $\MA$}, denoted $\oper\MA$, by $\oper\MA \defeq f$.
			\item We define the \tbf{type of $\MA$}, denoted $\typ\MA: J \rightarrow \N_0$, by $\typ\MA(j) \defeq \ar f_j$.
		\end{itemize}
	\end{defn}

	\begin{defn}
		Let $\MA$, $\MB$ be algebras. Then $\MA$ and $\MB$ are said to be \tbf{type-similar}, denoted $\MA \sim_{\typ} \MB$, if $\typ \MA = \typ \MB$.
	\end{defn}
	
	\begin{defn}
		Let $(A, f), (B, g)$ be algebras and $\al:A \rightarrow B$. Suppose that $(A, f) \sim_{\typ} (B, g)$. Set $J \defeq \dom f$ and $\rho \defeq \typ(A, f)$. Write $f = (f_j)_{j \in J}$ and $g = (g_j)_{j \in J}$. Then $\al$ is said to be an \tbf{$((A,f), (B,g))$-homomorphism} if for each $j \in J$, 
		$$\al \circ f_j = g_j \circ \al^{\rho(j)}.$$
	\end{defn}
	
	\begin{ex}
		Let $(A, f), (B, g), (C, h)$ be algebras and $\al:A \rightarrow B$, $\be:B \rightarrow C$. Suppose that $(A, f) \sim_{\typ} (B, g), (C, h)$. Set $J \defeq \dom f$ and $\rho \defeq \typ(A, f)$. Write $f = (f_j)_{j \in J}$, $g = (g_j)_{j \in J}$ and $h = (h_j)_{j \in J}$. If $\al$ is a $((A,f), (B,g))$-homomorphism and $\be$ is a $((B,g), (C,h))$-homomorphism, then $\be \circ \al$ is a $((A,f), (C,h))$-homomorphism.
	\end{ex}
	
	\begin{proof}
		Suppose that $\al$ is a $((A,f), (B,g))$-homomorphism and $\be$ is a $((B,g), (C,h))$-homomorphism. Let $j \in J$. Since $\al$ is a $((A,f), (B,g))$-homomorphism, $\al \circ f_j = g_j \circ \al^{\rho(j)}$. Since $\be$ is a $((B,g), (C,h))$-homomorphism, $\be \circ g_j = h_j \circ \be^{\rho(j)}$. Therefore
		\begin{align*}
			(\be \circ \al) \circ f_j
			& = \be \circ (\al \circ f_j) \\
			& = \be \circ (g_j \circ \al^{\rho(j)}) \\
			& = (\be \circ g_j) \circ \al^{\rho(j)} \\
			& = (h_j \circ \be^{\rho(j)}) \circ \al^{\rho(j)} \\
			& = h_j \circ (\be^{\rho(j)} \circ \al^{\rho(j)}) \\
			& = h_j \circ ([\be \circ \al]^{\rho(j)}).
		\end{align*}
		Since $j \in J$ is arbitrary, we have that for each $j \in J$ and $(\be \circ \al) \circ f_j = h_j \circ ([\be \circ \al]^{\rho(j)})$. Hence $\be \circ \al$ is a $((A,f), (C,h))$-homomorphism.
	\end{proof}
	
	\begin{ex}
		Let $(A, f)$ be and algebra. Set $J \defeq \dom f$. Write $f = (f_j)_{j \in J}$. Then $\id_A$ is a $((A,f), (A,f))$-homomorphism.
	\end{ex}
	
	\begin{proof}
		Since for each $j \in J$,
		\begin{align*}
			\id_A \circ f_j
			& = f_j \\
			& = f_j \circ \id_{A^{\rho(j)}} \\
			& = f_j \circ \id_A^{\rho(j)},
		\end{align*}
		we have that $\id_A$ is a $((A,f), (A,f))$-homomorphism.
	\end{proof}
	
	\begin{defn}\
		\begin{itemize}
			\item Let $J$ be a set and $\rho: J \rightarrow \N_0$. Then $\rho$ is said to be a \tbf{type function}. We define $\typs \defeq \{\rho: \text{$\rho$ is a type function}\}$.
			\item Let $\rho \in \typs$. We define the \tbf{category of algebras with type $\rho$}, denoted $\Alg_\rho$, by 
			\begin{itemize}
				\item $\Obj(\Alg_\rho) \defeq \{\MA: \text{$\MA$ is an algebra and $\typ \MA = \rho$}\}$
				\item For $(A,f), (B,g) \in \Obj(\Alg_\rho)$, $\Hom_{\Alg_{\rho}}(\MA, \MB) \defeq \{\al \in B^A: \text{$\al$ is a $(\MA, \MB)$-homomorphism.}\}$
			\end{itemize}
		\end{itemize}
	\end{defn}
	
	\begin{ex}
		Let $\rho \in \typs$. Then $\Alg_{\rho}$ is a category.
	\end{ex}
	
	\begin{proof}
		\tcr{FINISH!!!}
	\end{proof}
	
























































	\newpage
	\section{Subalgebras}
	
	\begin{defn}
		Let $\rho \in \typs$ and $\MA, \MB \in \Obj(\Alg_\rho)$. Write $\MA = (A, f)$ and $\MB = (B, g)$. Set $J \defeq \dom \rho$. Write $f = (f_j)_{j \in J}$ and $g = (g_j)_{j \in J}$. 
		\begin{itemize}
			\item Then $\MB$ is said to be a \tbf{subalgebra of $\MA$} if 
			\begin{enumerate}
				\item $B \subset A$ 
				\item $\iota_B \in \Hom_{\Alg_\rho}(\MB, \MA)$.
			\end{enumerate}
			\item We define $\suba(\MA) \defeq \{\MB \in \Obj(\Alg_\rho): \text{$\MA \sim_{\typ} \MB$ and $\MB$ is a subalgebra of $\MA$}\}$.
		\end{itemize}
	\end{defn}
	
	\begin{ex}
		Let $\rho \in \typs$ and $\MA, \MB \in \Obj(\Alg_\rho)$. Write $\MA = (A, f)$ and $\MB = (B, g)$. Set $J \defeq \dom \rho$. Write $f = (f_j)_{j \in J}$ and $g = (g_j)_{j \in J}$. Then $(B, g) \in \suba(A, f)$ is a subalgebra of $(A, f)$ iff 
		\begin{enumerate}
			\item $B \subset A$ 
			\item for each $j \in J$, $f_j|_{B^{\rho(j)}} = g_j$.
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{ex}
	
	\begin{proof}\
		\begin{itemize}
			\item $(\implies)$: \\
			Suppose that $\MB$ is a subalgebra of $\MA$. 
			\begin{enumerate}
				\item By definition, $B \subset A$. 
				\item Let $j \in J$. Since $\MB$ is a subalgebra of $\MA$, $\iota_B \in \Hom_{\Alg_\rho}(\MB, \MA)$. Then 
				\begin{align*}
					f_j|_{B^{\rho(j)}}
					& = f_j \circ \iota_{B^{\rho(j)}} \\
					& = f_j \circ \iota_B^{\rho(j)} \\
					& = \iota_B \circ g_j \\
					& = g_j.
				\end{align*} 
				Since $j \in J$ is arbitrary, we have that for each $j \in J$, $f_j|_{B^{\rho(j)}} = g_j$.
			\end{enumerate}
			\item $(\impliedby)$: \\
			Suppose that 
			\begin{enumerate}
				\item $B \subset A$ 
				\item for each $j \in J$, $f_j|_{B^{\rho(j)}} = g_j$.
			\end{enumerate}
			Let $j \in J$. Then 
			\begin{align*}
				\iota_B \circ g_j
				& = g_j \\
				& = f_j|_{B^{\rho(j)}} \\
				& = f_j \circ \iota_{B^{\rho(j)}} \\
				& = f_j \circ \iota_B^{\rho(j)}.
			\end{align*} 
			Since $j \in J$ is arbitary, we have that for each $j \in J$, $\iota_B \circ g_j = f_j \circ \iota_B^{\rho(j)}$. Thus $\iota_B \in \Hom_{\Alg_\rho}(\MB, \MA)$. 
			\begin{enumerate}
				\item $B \subset A$
				\item Let $j \in J$ and $a \in B^{\rho(j)}$. Then 
				\begin{align*}
					f_j(\iota_B^{\rho(j)}(a)) 
					& = f_j(\iota_{B^{\rho(j)}}(a)) \\
					& = f_j \circ \iota_{B^{\rho(j)}} (a) \\
					& = f_j|_{B^{\rho(j)}}(a) \\
					& = g_j(a) \\
					& = \iota_B(g_j(a)).
				\end{align*} 
			\end{enumerate}
		\end{itemize}
	\end{proof}
	
	\begin{defn}
		Let $\rho \in \typs$ and $(A, f), (B, g) \in \Obj(\Alg_\rho)$. Set $J \defeq \dom \rho$. Write $f = (f_j)_{j \in J}$ and $g = (g_j)_{j \in J}$. 
		\begin{itemize}
			\item Then $(B, g)$ is said to be a \tbf{subalgebra of $(A, f)$} if 
			\begin{enumerate}
				\item $B \subset A$ 
				\item for each $j \in J$, $f_j|_{B^{\rho(j)}} = g_j$.
			\end{enumerate}
			\item We define $\suba(\MA) \defeq \{\MB \in \Obj(\Alg_\rho): \text{$\MA \sim_{\typ} \MB$ and $\MB$ is a subalgebra of $\MA$}\}$.
		\end{itemize}
	\end{defn}
	
	\begin{defn}
		Let $\rho \in \typs$, $(A, f) \in \Obj(\Alg_\rho)$ and $B \in \Obj(\Set)$. 
		\begin{itemize}
			\item Then $B$ is said to be a \tbf{subuniverse of $(A, f)$} if 
			\begin{enumerate}
				\item $B \subset A$,
				\item $B$ is $\Im f$-closed.
			\end{enumerate}
			\item We define $\subu(\MA) \defeq \{B \in \Obj(\Set): \text{$B$ is a subuniverse of $\MA$}\}$.
		\end{itemize}
	\end{defn}
	
	\begin{ex}
		Let $\rho \in \typs$ and $(A, f), (B, g) \in \Obj(\Alg_{\rho})$. If $(B, g)$ is a subalgebra of $(A, f)$, then $B$ is a subuniverse of $(A,f)$. 
	\end{ex}
	
	\begin{proof}
		Set $J \defeq \dom \rho$ and $\rho \defeq \typ(A, f)$. Suppose that $(B, g)$ is a subalgebra of $(A, f)$. 
		\begin{enumerate}
			\item Since $(B, g)$ is a subalgebra of $(A, f)$, we have that $B \subset A$.
			\item Let $j \in J$. Then for each $a \in B^\rho(j)$, 
			\begin{align*}
				f_j(a_1, \ldots, a_{\rho(j)})
				& = f_j|_{B^{\rho(j)}}(a_1, \ldots, a_{\rho(j)}) \\
				& = g_j(a_1, \ldots, a_{\rho(j)}) \\
				& \in B.
			\end{align*}
			Since $j \in J$ is arbitrary, we have that $B$ is $\Im f$-closed. Thus $B$ is a subuniverse of $A$.
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{proof}

	\begin{defn}
		Let $\MA$ be an algebra and $B$ a subuniverse of $\MA$. Set $\MS(B, \MA) \defeq \{S \subset A: \text{$S$ is a subuniverse of $\MA$ and $B \subset S$}\}$. We define the \tbf{subuniverse of $\MA$ generated by $B$}, denoted $\Sg(B, \MA)$, by
		$$\Sg(B, \MA) \defeq \bigcap\limits_{S \in \MS(B, \MA)} S $$
	\end{defn}
	\tcr{show $\MS \neq \varnothing$ and intersection of subiniverses is subuniverse}

	\begin{ex}
		Let $(A, f)$ be an algebra and $B \subset A$. Then 
		\begin{enumerate}
			\item $\Sg(B, f)$ is a subuniverse of $A$
			\item $B \subset \Sg(B, f)$.
		\end{enumerate}.
	\end{ex}

	\begin{proof}\
		\begin{enumerate}
			\item Set $\MS \defeq \{S \subset A: \text{$S$ is an $f$-subuniverse of $A$}\}$. By construction, for each $S \in S$, $S$ is $f$-closed. Since $\Sg(B, f) = \bigcap\limits_{S \in \MS} S$, \rex{} \tcr{A previous exercise in the set theory section} implies that $\Sg(B, f)$ is $f$-closed. Hence $\Sg(B, f)$ is an $f$-subuniverse of $A$. 
			\item By construction, for each $S \in S$, $B \subset S$. Thus
			\begin{align*}
				B
				& \subset \bigcap\limits_{S \in \MS} S \\
				& = \Sg(B, f).
			\end{align*}
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{proof}

	\begin{ex}
		Let $(A, f)$ be an algebra. Then $\Sg(\cdot, f)$ is an algebraic closure operator on $A$.
	\end{ex}

	\begin{proof}
		
	\end{proof}

	

	

	



































\section{Congruences}

\begin{defn}
	Let $\rho \in \typs$ and $(A, f) \in \Obj(\Alg_\rho)$. Set $J \defeq \dom \rho$. Write $f = (f_j)_{j \in J}$. 
	\begin{itemize}
		\item Let $\th \in \Equi(A)$. Then $\th$ is said to be a \tbf{congruence on $(A, f)$} if for each $j \in J$ and $a, b \in A^{\rho(j)}$, $a \th^{\times \rho(j)} b$ implies that $f_j(a) \th f_j(b)$. 
		\item We define $\Cong(A,f) \defeq \{\th \in \Equi(A): \text{$\th$ is a congruence on $(A, f)$}\}$.
	\end{itemize}
\end{defn}

\begin{note}
	\tcr{Question}: $\th$ is congruence iff $f_j^{\times 2}(\th^{\times \rho(j)}) \subset \th $, is there a generalization of equivalence relation to $\th \subset A^n$ that is reflexive ($\Del_{A,n} \subset \th$) symmetric (invariant under $S_n$), and transitive (idk what the generalization of composition should be), then define congruence by $$f_j^{\times n}(\th^{\times \rho(j)}) \subset \th$$  \\
	
	when $n = 3$, the question becomes what meaning could capture the statement that a set of three objects 'has sameness', but no two element subset of them `has sameness` in the same way. 
\end{note}

\begin{ex}
	Let $\rho \in \typs$, $(A, f) \in \Obj(\Alg_\rho)$ and $\th \in \Cong(A, f)$. 
\end{ex}

\begin{proof}
	
\end{proof}



\begin{defn}
	Let $\MA \in \Obj(\Alg_\rho)$ and $\th \in \Cong(\MA)$. Write $\MA = (A, f)$, set $J \defeq \dom \rho$ and write $f = (f_j)_{j \in J}$. We define 
	\begin{itemize}
		\item $f^{\th} \in \MF^*(A/ \th )^J$ by 
		$f^{\th}_j \defeq \Fac_{\th^{\rho(j)}, \th}(f_j)$. 
		\item \tbf{quotient algebra of $\MA$ by $\th$}, denoted $\MA / \th$, by $\MA / \th \defeq (A /\th, f^{\th})$. 
	\end{itemize}
	
	
	\tcr{introduce factor notation here and apply it to analysis and other notes, aka if we fix $\pi_X:X \rightarrow X/\th_X$, $\pi_Y:Y \rightarrow Y/\th_Y$ and consider $f:X \rightarrow Y$ which is $(\th_X, \th_Y)$-invariant, then there exists a unique $\tl{f}:X/\th_X \rightarrow Y/\th_Y$ such that $\pi_Y \circ f = \tl{f} \circ \pi_{\th_X}$, we define $\Fac_{\th_X, \th_Y}(f) \defeq \tl{f}$}. \\
		
	\tcr{I guess we can define a $\Fac$ map or something similar in other contexts, i.e. for other universal properties/limits/colimits since they all have some unique map involved as a factor of some expression related to the original map.}\\
	
	\tcr{need to go over equiv relations in set theory notes}
\end{defn}

\begin{ex}
	Let $\rho \in \typ$, $\MA \in \Obj(\Alg_\rho)$ and $\th \in \Cong(\MA)$. Then $\MA / \th \in \Obj(\Alg_\rho)$. 
\end{ex}

\begin{proof}
	Write $\MA = (A, f)$. Set $J \defeq \dom \rho$. By assumption, $A,J \neq \varnothing$. Hence $(A/ \th, f^{\th})$ is an algebra. By definition, for each $j \in J$, $f_j \in \MF^j(A)$ iff $f^{\th}_j \in \MF^j(A/\th)$. Hence 
	\begin{align*}
		\typ (A/ \th, f^{\th})
		& = \typ (A, f) \\
		& = \rho. 
	\end{align*}
	Thus $(A/ \th, f^{\th}) \in \Obj(\Alg_{\rho})$.
\end{proof}



	
	

	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	










































 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 \appendix
 
 \chapter{Summation}
 
 \begin{defn} \ld{}
 	Let $f:X \rightarrow \Rg$, Then we define $$\sum_{x \in X} f(x) := \sup_{\substack{F \subset X \\ F \text{ finite}}} \sum_{x \in F} f(x)$$ This definition coincides with the usual notion of summation when $X$ is countable. For $f:X \rightarrow \C$, we can write $f = g +ih$ where $g,h:X \rightarrow \R$. If $$\sum_{x \in X}|f(x)| < \infty,$$ then the same is true for $g^+,g^-,h^+,h^-$. In this case, we may define $$\sum_{x \in X} f(x)$$ in the obvious way.
 \end{defn} 
 
 The following note justifies the notation $\sum_{x \in X}f(x)$ where $f:X \rightarrow \C$.
 
 \begin{note}
 	Let $f:X \rightarrow \C$ and $\al:X \rightarrow X$ a bijection. If $\sum_{x \in X}|f(x)|< \infty$, then $\sum_{x \in X}f( \al (x)) = \sum_{x \in X}f(x) $.
 \end{note}
 
 \newpage	
 
 \chapter{Asymptotic Notation}
 
 \begin{defn} \ld{}
 	Let $X$ be a topological space, $Y, Z$ be normed vector spaces, $f:X \rightarrow Y$, $g: X \rightarrow Z$ and $x_0 \in X \cup \{\infty\}$. Then we write $$f = o(g) \hspace{.5cm} \text{ as } x \rightarrow x_0$$ if for each $\ep >0$, there exists $U \in \MN(x_0)$ such that for each $x \in U$, $$\|f(x)\| \leq \ep\|g(x)\|$$
 \end{defn}
 
 \begin{ex} \lex{}
 	Let $X$ be a topological space, $Y, Z$ be normed vector spaces, $f:X \rightarrow Y$, $g: X \rightarrow Z$ and $x_0 \in X \cup \{\infty\}$. If there exists $U \in \MN(x_0)$ such that for each $x \in U \setminus \{x_0\}$, $g(x) > 0$, then $$f = o(g) \text{ as } x \rightarrow x_0 \hspace{.25cm} \text{ iff } \hspace{.25cm}  \lim_{x \rightarrow x_0} \frac{\| f(x) \|}{\| g(x) \|} = 0$$
 \end{ex}	
 
 \begin{ex} \lex{}
 	Let $X$ and $Y$ a be normed vector spaces, $A \subset X$ open and $f:A \rightarrow Y$. Suppose that $0 \in A$. If $f(h) = o(\|h\|)$ as $h \rightarrow 0$, then for each $h \in X$,  $f(th) = o(|t|)$ as $t \rightarrow 0$.
 \end{ex}	
 
 \begin{proof}
 	Suppose that $f(h) = o(\|h\|)$ as $h \rightarrow 0$.  Let $h \in X$ and $\ep >0$. Choose $\del' >0 $ such that for each $h' \in B(0, \del')$, $h' \in A$ and 
 	$$\|f(h')\| \leq \frac{\ep}{\|h\|+1} \|h'\|$$ 
 	Choose $\del >0$ such that for each $t \in B(0,\del)$, $th \in B(0,\del')$. Let $t \in B(0,\del)$. Then 
 	\begin{align*}
 		\|f(th)\| 
 		&\leq \frac{\ep}{\|h\|+1} |t|\|h\| \\
 		&< \ep |t|
 	\end{align*}
 	So $f(th) = o(|t|)$ as $t \rightarrow 0$.
 \end{proof}		
 
 
 
 
 \begin{defn} \ld{}
 	Let $X$ be a topological space, $Y, Z$ be normed vector spaces, $f:X \rightarrow Y$, $g: X \rightarrow Z$ and $x_0 \in X \cup \{\infty\}$. Then we write $$f = O(g) \hspace{.5cm} \text{ as } x \rightarrow x_0$$ if there exists $U \in \MN(x_0)$ and $M \geq 0$ such that for each $x \in U$, $$\|f(x)\| \leq M\|g(x)\|$$
 \end{defn}

 \chapter{Categories}
 
 \tcr{move to notation?}
 
 \begin{defn}
 	We define the category of topological measure spaces, denoted $\TopMsrpos$, by 
 	\begin{itemize}
 		\item $\Obj(\TopMsrpos) \defeq \{(X, \mu): X \in \Obj(\Top) \text{ and } \mu \in M(X)\}$			
 		\item $\Hom_{\TopMsrpos}((X, \mu), (Y, \nu)) \defeq \Hom_{\Top}(X, Y) \cap \Hom_{\Msrpos}((X, \MB(X), \mu), (Y, \MB(Y), \nu))$
 	\end{itemize}
 \end{defn}

 \chapter{Vector Spaces}
 \tcr{it might be better to cover some category theory and write everything in terms of $\Hom_{\VectK}$ and $\Obj(\VectK)$}
 
 \section{Introduction}
 
 \begin{defn}
 	Let $X$ be a set, $\K$ a field, $+:X \times X \rightarrow X$ and $\cdot:\K \times X \rightarrow X$. Then $(X, +, \cdot)$ is said to be a \tbf{$\K$-vector space} if 
 	\begin{enumerate}
 		\item $(X, +)$ is an abelian group
 		\item 
 	\end{enumerate} 
 \end{defn}
 
 
 \begin{defn}
 	Let $(X, +_X, \cdot_X)$ and $(E, +_E, \cdot_E)$ be vector spaces. Suppose that $E \subset X$. Then $(E, +_E, \cdot_E)$ is said to be a subspace of $X$ if 
 	\begin{enumerate}
 		\item $+_E = +_X|_{E \times E}$
 		\item $\cdot_E = \cdot_X|_{\K \times E}$
 	\end{enumerate}
 \end{defn}
 
 \begin{ex}
 	Let $(X, +_X, \cdot_X)$ and $(E, +_E, \cdot_E)$ be vector spaces. Suppose that $E \subset X$. 
 \end{ex}
 
 \begin{ex}
 	Let $(X, +, \cdot)$ be a vector space and $E \subset X$. Then $E$ is a subspace of $X$
 \end{ex}
 
 
 \begin{defn}
 	Let $X$ be a vector space and $(E_j)_{j \in J}$ a collection of subspaces of $X$. Then $\bigcap\limits_{j \in J}E_j$ is a subspace of $X$. 
 \end{defn}
 
 \begin{proof}
 	Set $E \defeq \bigcap\limits_{j \in J}E_j$. Let $x,y \in E$ and $\lam \in \K$. Then for each $j \in J$, $x,y \in E_j$. Since for each $j \in J$, $E_j$ is a subspace of $X$, we have that for each $j \in J$, $x+ \lam y \in E_j$. Thus $x+\lam y \in E$. Since $x,y \in E$ and $\lam \in \K$ are arbitrary, \tcr{(cite exercise here)} we have that $E$ is a subspace of $X$. 
 \end{proof}
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 \begin{defn}
 	Let $X, Y$ be vector spaces and $T:X \rightarrow Y$. Then $T$ is said to be \tbf{linear} if for each $x_1, x_2 \in X$ and $\lam \in \Lam$, 
 	\begin{enumerate}
 		\item $T(x_1 + x_2) = T(x_1) + T(x_2)$,
 		\item $T(\lam x_1) = \lam T(x_1)$.
 	\end{enumerate}
 	We define $L(X;Y) \defeq \{T:X \rightarrow Y: \text{ $T$ is linear}\}$. 
 \end{defn}
 
 \begin{ex}
 	Let $X,Y$ be vector spaces and $T : X \rightarrow Y$. Then $T$ is linear iff for each $x_1, x_2 \in X$ and $\lam \in \Lam$, 
 	$$T(x_1 + \lam x_2) = T(x_1) + \lam T(x_2)$$
 \end{ex}
 
 \begin{proof}
 	Clear. \tcr{(add details)}
 \end{proof}
 
 \begin{defn}
 	\tcr{define addition/scalar multiplication of linear maps}
 \end{defn}
 
 \begin{ex}
 	Let $X,Y$ be vector spaces. Then $L(X;Y)$ is a $\K$-vector space. 
 \end{ex}
 
 \begin{proof}
 	Clear
 \end{proof}
 
 \begin{defn} \ld{55001}\
 	Let $X$ be a vector space over $\K$ and $T :X \rightarrow \K$. Then $T$ is said to be a \tbf{linear functional on} $X$ if $T$ is linear. We define the \tbf{dual space of $X$}, denoted $X^*$, by $X^* \defeq \{ T:X \rightarrow \K: T \text{ is linear}\}$. 
 \end{defn}
 
 
 \begin{ex}
 	Let $X$ be a vector space. Then $X^*$ is a vector space. 
 \end{ex}
 
 \begin{proof}
 	Clear.
 \end{proof}
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 \section{Bases}
 
 \begin{defn}
 	Let $X$ be a vector space and $(e_{\al})_{\al \in A} \subset X$. Then $(e_{\al})_{\al \in A}$ is said to be
 	\begin{itemize}
 		\item \tbf{linearly independent} if for each $(\al_j)_{j=1}^n \subset A$, $(\lam_j)_{j=1}^n \subset \K$, $\sum\limits_{j=1}^n \lam_j e_{\al_j} = 0$ implies that for each $j \in [n]$, $\lam_j = 0$.  
 		\item a \tbf{Hamel basis for $X$} if $(e_{\al})_{\al \in A}$ is linearly independent and $\spn (e_{\al})_{\al \in A} = X$. 
 	\end{itemize}
 \end{defn}
 
 \begin{ex}
 	\tcr{every vector space has a Hamel basis}
 \end{ex}
 
 \begin{proof}
 	
 \end{proof}
 
 \begin{ex}
 	
 \end{ex}
 
 
 \begin{ex}
 	Let $X$ be a $\K$-vector space and $x \in X$. Then $x = 0$ iff for each $\phi \in X^*$, $\phi(x) = 0$. 
 \end{ex}
 
 \begin{proof}\
 	\begin{itemize}
 		\item $(\implies)$: \\
 		Suppose that $x = 0$. Linearity implies that for each $\phi \in X^*$ $\phi(x) = 0$. 
 		\item $(\impliedby)$: \\
 		Conversely, suppose that $x \neq 0$. Define $\ep_x: \spn(x) \rightarrow \K$ by $\ep_x(\lam x) \defeq \lam$. Let $u,v \in \spn(x)$. Then there exists $\lam_u, \lam_v \in \K$ such that $u = \lam_u x$ and $v = \lam_v x$. Suppose that $u = v$. Then 
 		\begin{align*}
 			(\lam_u - \lam_v)x
 			& = \lam_u x - \lam_v x \\
 			& = u - v \\
 			& = 0
 		\end{align*}
 		Since $x \neq 0$, we have that $\lam_u - \lam_v = 0$ and therefore $\lam_u = \lam_v$. Hence  
 		\begin{align*}
 			\lam_u 
 			& = \ep_x(u) \\
 			& = \ep_x(v) \\
 			& = \lam_v.
 		\end{align*}
 		Thus $\ep_x$ is well defined. 
 	\end{itemize}
 \end{proof}
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 \newpage
 \section{Multilinear Maps}
 
 \begin{defn}
 	Let $X_1, \cdots, X_n, Y$ be vector spaces and $T: \prod\limits_{j=1}^n X_j \rightarrow \K$. Then $T$ is said to be \tbf{multilinear} if for each $j_0 \in [n]$ and $(x_j)_{j=1}^n \in \prod\limits_{j=1}^n X_j$, $T(x_1, \ldots, x_{j_0 - 1}, \cdot, x_{j_0 + 1})$ is linear. $$L^n(X_1, \dots, X_n; Y) = \bigg\{T : \prod\limits_{j=1}^n X_j \rightarrow Y: T \text{ is multilinear}\bigg \}$$ 
 	If $X_1 = \cdots = X_n = X$, we write $L^n(X;Y)$ in place of $L^n (X, \dots, X; Y) $. 
 \end{defn}
 
 \begin{defn}
 	\tcr{define addition and scalar mult of multilinear maps}
 \end{defn}
 
 \begin{ex}
 	Let $X_1, \cdots, X_n, Y$ be vector spaces. Then $L^n(X_1, \ldots, X_n;Y)$ is a $\K$-vector space.
 \end{ex}
 
 \begin{proof}
 	content...
 \end{proof}
 
 \begin{ex}
 	Let $X_1, \cdots, X_n, Y, Z$ be $\K$-vector spaces, $\al \in L^n(X_1, \ldots, X_n;Y)$ and $\phi \in L^1(Y;Z)$. Then $\phi \circ \al \in L^n(X_1, \ldots, X_n; Z)$. 
 \end{ex}
 
 \begin{proof}
 	Let $(x_j)_{j=1}^n \in \prod\limits_{j=1}^n X_j$ and $j_0 \in [n]$. Define $f:X_{j_0} \rightarrow Y$ by 
 	$$f(a) \defeq \al(x_1, \ldots, x_{j_0-1}, a , x_{j_0+1}, \ldots, x_n) $$
 	Since $\al \in L^n(X_1, \ldots, X_n;Y)$, $f$ is linear. Since $\phi$ is linear, and $\phi \circ f$ is linear. Since $(x_j)_{j=1}^n \in \prod\limits_{j=1}^n X_j$ and $j_0 \in [n]$ are arbitrary, we have that $\phi \circ \al \in L^n(X_1, \ldots, X_n;Y)$. 
 \end{proof}
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 \newpage
 \section{Tensor Products}
 
 \begin{defn}
 	Let $X, Y$ and $T$ be vector spaces over $\K$ and $\al \in L^2(X, Y; T)$. Then $(T, \al)$ is said to be a \tbf{tensor product of $X$ and $Y$} if for each vector space $Z$ and $\be \in L^2(X, Y; Z)$, there exists a unique $\phi \in L^1(T;Z)$ such that $\phi \circ \al = \be$, i.e. the following diagram commutes:
 	\[ 
 	\begin{tikzcd}
 		X \times Y \arrow[r, "\al"] \arrow[dr, "\be"'] 	
 		& T  \arrow[d, dashed, "\phi"] \\
 		& Z 
 	\end{tikzcd}
 	\] 
 \end{defn}
 
 \begin{ex}
 	Let $X, Y, S, T$ be vector spaces, $\al \in L^2(X, Y; S)$ and $\be \in L^2(X, Y; T)$. Suppose that $(S, \al)$ and $(T, \be)$ are tensor products of $X$ and $Y$. Then $S$ and $T$ are isomorphic. 
 \end{ex}
 
 \begin{proof}
 	Since $(T, \be)$ is a tensor product of $X$ and $Y$, $\be \in L^2(X,Y; T)$ there exists a unique $f \in L^1(T;T)$ such that $f\ circ \be = \be$, i.e. the following diagram commutes: 
 	\[ 
 	\begin{tikzcd}
 		& T \arrow[dd, dashed, "f"] \\
 		X \times Y \arrow[ur, "\be"] \arrow[dr, "\be"'] 
 		&   \\
 		& T 
 	\end{tikzcd}
 	\] 
 	Since $\id_T \in L^1(T;T)$ and $\id_T \circ \be = \be$, we have that $f = \id_T$. Since $(S, \al)$ is a tensor product of $X$ and $Y$, there exists a unique $\phi: S \rightarrow T$ such that $\phi \circ \al = \be$, i.e. the following diagram commutes: 
 	\[ 
 	\begin{tikzcd}
 		X \times Y \arrow[r, "\al"] \arrow[dr, "\be"'] 	
 		& S  \arrow[d, dashed, "\phi"] \\
 		& T 
 	\end{tikzcd}
 	\] 
 	Similarly, since $(T, \be)$ is a tensor product of $X$ and $Y$, there exists a unique $\psi: T \rightarrow S$ such that $\psi \circ \be = \al$, i.e. the following diagram commutes: 
 	\[ 
 	\begin{tikzcd}
 		X \times Y \arrow[r, "\be"] \arrow[dr, "\al"'] 	
 		& T  \arrow[d, dashed, "\psi"] \\
 		& S 
 	\end{tikzcd}
 	\] 
 	Therefore 
 	\begin{align*}
 		(\phi \circ \psi) \circ \be 
 		& = \phi \circ (\psi \circ \be) \\
 		& = \phi \circ \al \\
 		& = \be, 
 	\end{align*}
 	i.e. the following diagram commutes:
 	\[ 
 	\begin{tikzcd}
 		& T \arrow[d, dashed, "\psi"] \\
 		X \times Y \arrow[ur, "\be"] \arrow[dr, "\be"'] \arrow[r, "\al"]	
 		& S  \arrow[d, dashed, "\phi"] \\
 		& T 
 	\end{tikzcd}
 	\implies
 	\begin{tikzcd}
 		& T \arrow[dd, dashed, "\phi \circ \psi"] \\
 		X \times Y \arrow[ur, "\be"] \arrow[dr, "\be"'] 
 		&  \\
 		& T 
 	\end{tikzcd}
 	\] 
 	By uniqueness of $f \in L^1(T;T)$, we have that 
 	\begin{align*}
 		\id_T
 		& = f \\
 		& = \phi \circ \psi 
 	\end{align*}
 	A similar argument implies that $\psi \circ \phi = \id_S$. Hence $\phi$ and $\psi$ are isomorphisms with $\phi^{-1} = \psi$. Hence $S$ and $T$ are isomorphic.
 \end{proof}
 
 \begin{defn}
 	Let $X, Y$ be vector spaces, $x \in X$ and $y \in Y$. We define $x \otimes y: X^* \times Y^* \rightarrow  \K$ by $x \otimes y(\phi, \psi) \defeq \phi(x) \psi(y)$.  
 \end{defn}
 
 \begin{ex}
 	Let $X, Y$ be vector spaces, $x \in X$ and $y \in Y$. Then $x \otimes y \in L^2(X^*, Y^*; \K)$. 
 \end{ex}
 
 \begin{proof}
 	Let $\phi_1, \phi_2 \in X^*$, $\psi \in Y^*$ and $\lam \in \K$. Then 
 	\begin{align*}
 		x \otimes y(\phi_1 + \lam \phi_2, \psi) 
 		& = [\phi_1 + \lam \phi_2 (x)] \psi(y) \\
 		& = \phi_1(x)\psi(y) + \lam \phi_2(x)\psi(y) \\
 		& = x \otimes y(\phi_1, \psi) + \lam x \otimes y(\phi_2, \psi)
 	\end{align*}
 	Since $\phi_1, \phi_2 \in X^*$, $\psi \in Y^*$ and $\lam \in \K$ are arbitrary, we have that for each $\psi \in Y^*$, $x \otimes y(\cdot, \psi)$ is linear. Similarly for each $\phi \in X^*$, $x \otimes y(\phi, \cdot)$ is linear. Hence $x \otimes y$ is bilinear and $x \otimes y \in L^2(X^*, Y^*; \K)$. 
 \end{proof}
 
 \begin{defn}
 	Let $X, Y$ be vector spaces. We define  
 	\begin{itemize}
 		\item the \tbf{tensor product of $X$ and $Y$}, denoted $X \otimes Y \subset L^2(X^*, Y^*; \K)$, by 
 		$$X \otimes Y \defeq \spn(\text{$x \otimes y: x \in X$ and $y \in Y$}),$$
 		\item the \tbf{tensor map}, denoted $\otimes: X \times Y \rightarrow X \otimes Y$, by $\otimes(x, y) \defeq x \otimes y$.
 	\end{itemize}
 \end{defn}
 
 \begin{ex}
 	Let $X,Y$ be vector spaces, $(x_j)_{j=1}^n \subset X$ and $(y_j)_{j=1}^n \subset Y$. The following are equivalent:
 	\begin{enumerate}
 		\item $\sum\limits_{j=1}^n  x_j \otimes y_j = 0$
 		\item for each $\phi \in X^*$ and $\psi \in Y^*$, $\sum\limits_{j=1}^n  \phi(x_j) \psi(y_j) = 0$
 		\item for each $\phi \in X^*$, $\sum\limits_{j=1}^n  \phi(x_j)  y_j = 0$
 		\item for each $\psi \in Y^*$, $\sum\limits_{j=1}^n  \psi(y_j) x_j = 0$
 	\end{enumerate}
 \end{ex}
 
 \begin{proof}\
 	\begin{enumerate}
 		\item $(1) \implies (2):$ \\
 		Suppose that $\sum\limits_{j=1}^n x_j \otimes y_j = 0$. Let $\phi \in X^*$ and $\psi \in Y^*$. Then 
 		\begin{align*}
 			\sum\limits_{j=1}^n  \phi(x_j) \psi(y_j)
 			& = \phi \bigg( \sum\limits_{j=1}^n \psi(y_j) x_j \bigg) \\
 			& = 
 		\end{align*}
 		\item 
 		\item 
 	\end{enumerate}
 \end{proof}
 
 \begin{ex}
 	Let $X, Y$ be vector spaces. Then $(X \otimes Y, \otimes)$ is a tensor product of $X$ and $Y$. 
 \end{ex}
 
 \begin{proof}
 	Let $Z$ be a vector space and $\al \in L^2(X, Y; Z)$. Define $\phi: X \otimes Y \rightarrow Z$ by $\phi \bigg( \sum\limits_{j=1}^n \lam_j x_j \otimes y_j) \defeq \sum\limits_{j =1}^n \lam_j \al(x_j, y_j)$.  
 	\begin{itemize}
 		\item \tbf{(well defined):} \\
 		Let $u \in X \otimes Y$. Then there exist $(\lam_j)_{j=1}^n \subset \K$, $(x_j)_{j=1}^n \subset X$, $(y_j)_{j=1}^n \subset Y$ such that $u = \sum\limits_{j=1}^n \lam_j x_j \otimes y_j$. Suppose that $u = 0$. Let $\phi \in Z^*$. Then $\phi \circ \al \in L^2(X,Y; Z)$.  
 	\end{itemize}
 \end{proof}
